# Mollusc Shell

Matthew G. Canti

Historic England, Eastney, United Kingdom

# 3.1 Biological Characteristics

Mollusc shells found on archaeological sites worldwide are mostly either from bivalves (which includes mussels, clam, oysters, etc.) or gastropods, which include the land snails. Their shells consist of a tough chitinous outer coating (periostracum), overlying layers of calcium carbonate, laid down by a secretory epithelium. Archaeological thin-section fragments have little visible organic material and therefore consist largely of oriented masses of crystals in layers cut at random angles across a wide range of shapes according to the animal's growth pattern. The crystal layers that make up the shell are themselves very variable, having been classified as homogeneous, prismatic, foliated, nacreous, grained, crossed lamellar, or complex by Bøggild (1930), with subsequent modifications by numerous other authors (see Watabe 1988).

# 3.2 Optical and Morphological Properties

Mollusc-shell carbonate can be aragonite or calcite, and sometimes both occur in the same shell (Simkiss & Wilbur 1989). The two are very difficult to distinguish optically. The different structures mostly show bunched, near-parallel, elongated crystal alignments, sometimes with multiple layers oriented in different directions (Figures 3.1 and 3.2).

The nacreous structural type, however, is distinctive. It is made of thin horizontal layers, each composed of tabular aragonite crystals deposited on a sheet of organic matrix lying almost parallel to the inner shell surface (Watabe 1988). The C axes of the crystals are perpendicular to the surface. Interference set up by the microtopography of the tabular crystals (Figure 3.3) produces the 'mother-of-pearl' iridescence in reflected light.

In addition to producing the shell itself, many gastropods seal the opening with a more-or-less mineralized protein seal known as an operculum (or epiphragm). The structure is built up of concentric layers starting from a central boss. Crystallization consists of rather irregular growths on the inside of the operculum, but towards the outside (away from the secreting animal) vertically arranged individual crystals develop of around  $1 - 4\mu \mathrm{m}$  (Bandel 1990). Thin sections are mostly indeterminate calcareous material, but some cuts near the edge yield subparallel layout of  $50 - 150\mu \mathrm{m}$  thick crystals (Figure 3.4).

The chemical weathering of shell is pH dependent, in common with other calcareous fossils. An unusual stage in the weathering sequence has recently been reported from Tierra del Fuego. Villagran & Poch (2014) showed the production of a form of needle-fibre calcite, released as microstructures peel away from the shell of Mytilus edulis (Figure 3.5, see also Villagran et al. 2011, Figure 5C). It is not yet known how widespread this process is (for example, in different climates).

# 3.3 Examples

In the past, molluscs were used for food and the shells for decoration and occasionally for currency. Sometimes, where suitable limestone was unavailable, shells were burnt as a raw material for making lime (e.g. Murphy 1992). The effects of heating on mollusc shells are discussed by Canti (2017, this book).

In most archaeological situations, however, shells are found as part of a discard accumulation, and these have occasionally been examined microscopically. Villagran et al. (2011), for example, studied shell midden deposits on the coast of Tierra del Fuego, with the aim of using micromorphology to determine functional areas in a settlement of the indigenous Yamana people (Figure 3.6).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/3fe2af1083bd0c67ae626376d910ec69869d8bdbc14f9f2509541b9a88bf1e00.jpg)  
Figure 3.1 Bivalve shell showing different crystal alignments in layers. Neolithic pile dwelling site of Risch-Aabach, Lake Zug, Switzerland. (a) PPL; (b) XPL. Image courtesy of K. Ismail-Meyer.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/ab974dd62dc0f7da831df27cc8b4c326279d28789d4b5b886a3763aaaa47bd0a.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/30ca9b2b3e0744da9c30fffcc8045cb5dd870096f3fabcca656140ceaa411fb8.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/8c4f3bdc7ff44b67f2be4abfa6b5e7b718fd8c21bbc55aa388cf36ff23a0ff2b.jpg)  
Figure 3.4 Gastropod operculi from common garden snail (Helix aspersa) set in sand. XPL.

They were able to identify some mollusc shells to class or genus in thin sections, based on morphology and colouring, and to determine the shells' heating history by comparison with modern experimental material. These results, along with other micromorphological characteristics, resulted in a combined microfacies model for the habitation area.

Linstadter & Kehl (2012) examined a rich archaeological coastal sequence at Ifri Oudadane in north-eastern Morocco. Thin sections (Figure 3.7) were made at a number of locations up through  $2\mathrm{m}$  of deposits spanning 5000 years from Epipalaeolithic to late Neolithic times. The deposits record the change from hunter-gatherers to food producers, and include shells and shell fragments from Ostriche, land snails, and marine molluscs, some of which were burnt.

# 3.4 Conclusions

Although shell identification and analysis will inevitably be carried out as part of the molluscan studies at most archaeological sites, micromorphological examination

can have a significant role where relationships with deposit accumulation are being examined. This requires a moderate level of identification, so as to provide the link to the more detailed zoological studies, and thus produce a more holistic view of the site formation.

# Biospheroids Produced by Earthworms

Matthew G. Canti

Historic England, Eastney, United Kingdom

# 4.1 Introduction

The term 'biospheroid' was used by Becze-Deak et al. (1997) to describe certain radially crystallized calcium carbonate granules that they found in loess soils and ascribed to earthworms. Although the granules had been described previously (e.g. Ponomareva 1948; Weicek & Messenger 1972; Bal 1977; Meijer 1985), some authors still interpreted these features as the product of arionid slugs. Subsequently Canti (1998) showed by experimentation that the biospheroids came only from earthworms. They derive from the calciferous glands (a well-known feature of most earthworm genera) and are produced in prodigious quantities on a daily basis (Canti 2007a). The calcium carbonate granules from slugs of the genus Arion, by contrast, are only released into the soil on death, and are consequently very rare.

# 4.2 Morphological and Optical Characteristics

Although calcium carbonate is produced by most earthworm species, the cohesion and size of the excreted product varies. In northern European soils, it is only worms of the genus Lumbricus that produce significant numbers of large coherent granules (Canti & Piearce 2003). The common form is a  $0.5 - 2\mathrm{mm}$  ovoid with an exterior composed of tightly packed (usually  $50 - 250\mu \mathrm{m}$ ) prismatic crystals, each showing two or three crystal faces (Figure 4.1 and Figure 4.2). Thin sectioned examples often have a roughly radial structure with many elongated  $50\times 250\mu \mathrm{m}$  crystals pointing outwards, and irregular crystals filling the wedge-shaped gaps. Some granules have a central mass of roughly equidimensional  $20 - 60\mu \mathrm{m}$  crystals (Figure 4.3, left-hand granule).

The range of sizes produced includes material right down to a few single crystals (Figure 4.4 and Figure 4.5). At these sizes, the distinction between earthworm-derived and other forms of calcite will inevitably be uncertain.

# 4.3 Preservation Potential

Granules are likely to be present in any circumneutral biologically active soil, but the numbers preserved will depend on ecological and taphonomic details. If that soil becomes buried by archaeological processes, a long-term pH in the stratigraphy of higher than about 7.5 will commonly lead to good preservation of granules. The larger  $(>0.5\mathrm{mm})$  granules will mainly be found in moist, moderately calcareous situations, where the soil textures are finer (more silt, clay or both) than loamy sand, with higher numbers occurring where the clay content is above ca.  $15\%$  (personal observation).

Buried whole soil profiles should show a distinct peak in granule numbers near the old land surface due to worms' continuous deposition of casts onto that surface before it was buried. This may not apply in situations of alluvial deposition where concentration patterns could be affected by alluvial sorting processes.

# 4.4 Archaeological Examples

Although the granules have been described many times from thin sections, there has rarely been any interpretation arising from those descriptions. Numerical and isotopic methods offer greater potential for providing archaeological information in the form of depth counts and dating measurements. Preece et al. (1995, 1998)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/039817cb1274732fde3af3d046b69e81181cd216183190695c697bbc95121739.jpg)

used the up-profile concentration pattern that occurs in natural soils to pinpoint buried soil horizons at Holywell Coombe and Watcombe Bottom, United Kingdom.

Dating approaches are still in their infancy, but Canti (2007b) showed that a group of radiocarbon dates from granules inside Silbury Hill, United Kingdom, reflected the true construction date of the central turf stack, after which emplacement of the enormous outer mass of the prehistoric mound prevented subsequent contamination with more recent granules. Canti et al. (2015) further showed that granule dates from a Mesolithic midden at Westward Ho!, United Kingdom, matched secure independent dates of the deposit and surrounding stratigraphy.

# 4.5 Arion Granules

Calcium carbonate granules can be found in the skin of the slug Arion ater (Figure 4.6), and will remain in the soil after the animal dies. They are single or multiple crystals and have a more-or-less irregular form. For further illustrations, see Canti (1998).

# 5

# Faecal Spherulites

Matthew G. Canti<sup>1</sup> and Jacques Élie Brochier<sup>2</sup>

$^{1}$  Historic England, Eastney, United Kingdom  
$^{2}$  Aix-Marseille Université (AMU), UMR 7269 LAMPEA, Aix-en-Provence, France

# 5.1 Introduction

Faecal spherulites are distinctive microscopic crystalline calcium carbonate features found in animal dung (Figure 5.1). They are frequently preserved in areas of alkaline sediment or in situations where dissolution is minimal. They can be valuable indicators of the presence of animals, especially domestic or wild herbivores (Brochier 1983), and in combination with suitable additional data, of domestication and herd management. However, arriving at such conclusions is not straightforward as wild animals frequently rest in the entrances of caves or rock shelters where they are responsible for spherulite accumulation (Cremaschi & Trombino 1999; Brochier 2007).

# 5.2 Biological Characteristics

Faecal spherulites are produced in the digestive tract of many animals. They are absent from the stomach, but crystallize in the first few centimetres of the small intestine (Brochier et al. 1992), probably mediated by bacterial precipitation (Buczynski & Chafetz 1991; Verrecchia et al. 1995; Chekroun et al. 2004). Particularly high concentrations are produced by ruminant herbivores (Canti 1999; Korstanje 2002; Coil et al. 2003). The presence or absence of spherulites in modern dung samples is not predictable. Although higher numbers are generally found where animals are grazing on alkaline pastures, samples from the whole pH range also regularly contain no spherulites (Canti 1999). Thus, it has been observed that flocks of the same

subspecies on the same geology may or may not produce spherulites (Brochier et al. 1992).

# 5.3 Optical Characteristics

Faecal spherulites are typically  $5 - 15\mu \mathrm{m}$  in size, and are composed of radially crystallized calcium carbonate. The very small crystal size and layout combine to produce a roughly circular object showing low-order interference colours with a fixed cross of extinction (i.e., a polar-oriented dark cross that stays approximately in position regardless of stage rotation). The majority of spherulites have diameters of less than  $10\mu \mathrm{m}$ , so their interference colours attain only low-order white through to red under crossed polars (Figure 5.2). Those reaching larger sizes can sometimes show second-order colours, including blue and green, occurring typically in a band a little more than half way out from the spherulite's centre (Figure 5.3). It should be noted, here, that a group of randomly dispersed spherulites viewed in thin section will often contain some examples that have been cut through the middle and therefore show much lower interference colours than would be expected from a simple thickness-retardation relationship (see Canti 1998, p. 436).

The cross polarized view of a single spherulite effectively mimics the interference figure commonly used in optical mineral identification, and with a quarter-wave plate inserted (Figure 5.4), faecal spherulites are pseudouniaxial negative (Canti 1998).

Although spherulites are generally distinctive, there are a number of microscopic features that show some similar morphological characteristics, including coccoliths

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/1a5aeea8054f1f8ae069a91d11f67330751a1f92c9dcd6a3a6e69298de6d7a8e.jpg)  
Figure 5.1 Faecal spherulites (including darker burned examples). Note some large detrital quartz grains mixed in. Rock shelter in the Upper Ebro Basin (North Spain). XPL. Image courtesy of Ana Polo Diaz.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/826c354ae328e4c925ee25f9282449fa14ad1cd63f2321044edba9d922cbee79.jpg)  
Figure 5.3 A large spherulite from modern dung, showing interference colours up to second-order green. The first-order grey or white objects mainly on the left of the picture are cellulose fibres. XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/30fff169b8845fd985f7bb5b5720dd256acd357f983a7015ca144e18fe8cab40.jpg)  
Figure 5.2 Typical small spherulites from modern dung, showing low order white (rendered yellowish by organic stains) through to red. The first-order grey or white linear features in the lower part of the picture are cellulose fibres. XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/636aa767e7f71ec68c7d3ef62825b1d54cd3cfd86ede0be63eaa88aa88cc56dc.jpg)  
Figure 5.4 Faecal spherulites in XPL with a quarter-wave plate inserted. Note that blue appears in the NW-SE quadrants of the spherulites and yellow in the NE-SW, mimicking the uniaxial negative interference figure of optical mineralogy. Fibres are again present, showing first-order orange and blue interference colours.

(Figure 5.5), bird urine and some slide manufacturing faults (see Canti 1998).

Darkened spherulites are a feature regularly found in archaeological stratigraphy (see Figure 5.1). They were first described in layers of burned manure from subrecent deposits in some Sicilian caves (Brochier et al. 1992) and have subsequently been recorded in a number of archaeological contexts (Brochier 1993; Iaconis & Boschian 2007; Castel et al. 2008; Polo Diaz 2010). Their colour is somewhere in a range between brown and slightly transparent through to black and completely

opaque. If the microscope light source is raised to high levels of brightness, the interference colours and extinction cross can still be seen, albeit obscured by the brown tone (Brochier 1996, 2002). With a  $\lambda$  plate inserted (Figure 5.6), the outermost thin band displays the blue and yellow of the pseudo-uniaxial negative figure (see above).

Brochier (1996, 2002) found that the darkening could be reproduced by experimental burning of sheep dung in an oxidizing atmosphere at  $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , and attributed it to carbonization of the internal organic component of the spherulites.

# 5.4 Preservation Potential

Owing to their tiny size, faecal spherulites are easily dissolved and can only survive in archaeological stratigraphy where alkaline conditions are maintained or where water throughflow is minimal (Brochier et al. 1992). They are, therefore, most commonly concentrated in caves and rock shelters. However, they are also found in open areas characterized by rapid burial, aridity or a high pH in the sediments (Brochier

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/3e9bf674af46341cfa1e1cb1fb4b15946fd0311e716725b5479441b1a81c2814.jpg)  
Figure 5.5 Coccoliths from ground Kimmeridge shale, a Jurassic coccolith-bearing rock. XPL.

1984; Badalyan et al. 2010; Castel et al. 2014; Shahack-Gross et al. 2014).

Spherulites are destroyed by heating above a certain temperature. This is between  $500 - 560^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  according to Brochier (2002), and  $650 - 700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  according to Shahack-Gross (2011). The difference suggests that other factors are at work in the thermal breakdown of spherulites.

# 5.5 Archaeological Examples

Spherulites have been very valuable for looking at the detail of animal-human interactions including domestication (Brochier 1993), the use of caves for herding, and the burning of dung (Brochier et al. 1992; Boschian & Montagnari-Kokelj 2000). When additional observations are interwoven with the spherulite data, further deductions and exclusions become possible. For example, the presence or the absence of spherulites (combined with siliceous algae) inside or outside large stone constructions on the Syrian steppe has proved their pastoral usage (Castel et al. 2008). At Althiburos in northern Tunisia, combined spherulite and phytolith analysis has provided insights into the detail of animal keeping and domestic life (Portillo & Albert 2011). Specifically, concentrations of inflorescence or whole-plant phytoliths, in association with spherulites, provided information on feeding style and seasonality, whereas spherulites in oven ash showed the use of dung as fuel (see also Gur-Arieh et al. 2014). Shahack-Gross and Finkelstein (2008) were similarly able to distinguish indoor ash deposits derived through dung and wood burning from external dungeon deposits by using micromorphology, phytoliths and spherulite analysis on an Iron Age site in the Negev Highlands of Israel.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/2009fcc30a0a6f62872629ac5884444e87b6e78f11002464458f36d2928ae9df.jpg)  
Figure 5.6 Cluster of spherulites with some darkened examples in PPL (a), XPL (b) and with  $\lambda$  plate inserted (c) to show the remnant of the pseudo-uniaxial negative colours on the perimeter.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/0f6295c23169b891b5631a553ddddf9cd3e054b7fdcd44ec51cfc3308b27f561.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/e505fa87c346f2ff569547ad971bc26754eeeb7f02ef94a479d216ae3670385d.jpg)

# 6

# Excrements of Herbivores

David Bronnimann<sup>1</sup>, Kristin Ismail-Meyer<sup>1</sup>, Philippe Rentzel<sup>1</sup>, Christine Pümpin<sup>1</sup> and Lenka Lisá<sup>2</sup>

<sup>1</sup> IPAS – Integrative Prehistory and Archaeological Science, University of Basel, Switzerland  
$^{2}$  Institute of Geology, Czech Academy of Science, Czech Republic

# 6.1 Introduction

Since the 1980s, coprolites in different stages of preservation have been identified by micromorphological analysis (Goldberg 1980; Brochier 1983; Courty et al. 1989). Today, the combination of micromorphology and other procedures (e.g., chemistry, palaeoparasitology, palynology and archaeobotanical analysis) leads to a better understanding and identification of faecal remains in archaeological soils and sediments (Akeret & Jacomet 1997; Akeret & Rentzel 2001; Le Bailly et al. 2006; Goldberg & Macphail 2006; Macphail & Crowther 2008; Goldberg et al. 2009; Kuhn et al. 2013; Pichler et al. 2014).

With the help of micromorphological analysis, coprolites and weathered coprogenic materials can not only be recognized, but can often also be associated with the former producers. Thus shape, preservation, embedded components (e.g., plant remains, bone fragments) and groundmass characteristics allow distinction between herbivore excrements and the coprolites of omnivores or carnivores (Courty et al. 1989; Macphail & Goldberg 2010; Bronnimann et al. 2017, this book).

Herbivore dung remains may occur in a wide range of onsite archaeological features but are also known from offsite situations (Macphail et al. 1998; Cruise et al. 2009; Macphail & Goldberg 2010).

# 6.2 Micromorphology

Fresh herbivore excrements are usually porous, loosely packed and consist mainly of poorly digested to undigested plant fragments, which are sometimes embedded in a brown to dark brown amorphous organic groundmass. On the basis of plant fragment size and texture, it is possible to distinguish between cattle, goat/sheep, horses and herbivorous pigs in many cases. Below, the

excrements of cattle, goat/sheep and horse, the most frequent domestic animals, are discussed in detail. Less common faecal remains of rodents (rabbit, house mouse) are treated more briefly. The following descriptions are based upon observations of material in thin sections from different archaeological soil samples as well as of modern reference material and on literature.

# 6.2.1 Excrements of Ruminants and Horses

Dung remains of ruminants and horses are the most common faecal features in archaeology, where layers composed mainly of dung remains can be observed in a variety of locations and contexts. They occur as layered, often burnt (charred or completely ashed) cave or rock shelter sediments (see Beeching & Moulin 1983; Brochier 1983; Wattez et al. 1990; Courty et al. 1991; Brochier et al. 1992, 1999; Macphail et al. 1997; Boschian & Montagnari-Kokelj 2000; Brochier & Claustre 2000; Karkanas 2006; Angelucci et al. 2009; Linstadter & Kehl 2012), as well preserved, organic dung layers in lake shore settlements (Akeret & Rentzel 2001; Ismail-Meyer & Rentzel 2004; Ismail-Meyer et al. 2013), as stable or enclosure crusts (Guelat et al. 1998; Shahack-Gross et al. 2003; Macphail et al. 2004; Shahack-Gross & Finkelstein 2008, Dejmal et al. 2014) or as crust fragments in middens or in manured soils (Macphail et al. 1998; Macphail & Goldberg 2010).

Ruminant and horse excrements mainly consist of poorly digested plant fragments (Figures 6.1, 6.2, 6.3, 6.4 and 6.5). They are normally brown to dark brown in PPL, pale brown under OIL and mainly isotropic in XPL. In recent herbivore excrements, cellulose is well preserved and shows interference colours under XPL (and also a strong autofluorescence under UV light - Macphail & Crowther 2008, Venclova et al. 2012), whereas completely preserved silica phytolith strands may appear greyish, probably due to enclosed

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/78a49c1579c2564d7ebd826fbd46f6cf196df74abfa65729089347109b99217a.jpg)  
Figure 6.1 (a) Highland cattle dung. The fibrous plant fragments may be several millimetres long. There is no evident layering. Beside grass stems (Poaceae; arrow), several leaf fragments of salix were detected by archaeobotanical analysis. Modern sample. Petit Camargue Alsacienne (France). Sampling: Ö. Akeret. PPL. (b) Same as (a), XPL. Note the interference colours of cellulose.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/1c3c39e769f5982fe2de4d5396478729fb4060984f1a2ba4e1c458e46ce8952a.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/cf68336f75490ba9cb67ab6dea46321d1b89e4937b590bedb72b48df4ea8f34d.jpg)  
Figure 6.2 Domestic cattle dung. This 6-month-old, flattened cattle excrement was collected after snowmelt collected in the Alps (Gemmipass, Switzerland) and is slightly weathered. Note the distinct layering of the long plant fibres (mainly Poaceae fragments). PPL. Inset: Thin section scan of whole dung sample (scale bar length  $1\mathrm{cm}$ ).

particles (Figure 6.3). The composition of the organic matter varies significantly and largely depends on the animals' diet. Excrements usually consist of digested stems, leaves, twigs, seeds, bark, pollen and spores (Akeret & Jacomet 1997; Akeret et al. 1999; Akeret & Rentzel 2001; Shahack-Gross 2011; Ismail-Meyer et al. 2013; Kuhn et al. 2013; Dejmal et al. 2014) (Figures 6.1, 6.2, 6.4 and 6.5). In fresh dung, calcium oxalate crystals and dung spherulites can be observed regularly (Figure 6.3). With the exception of grasses (Poaceae), calcium oxalate crystals occur in most plants - especially in the leaves of dicotyledonous plants (Prychid & Rudall 1999; Franceschi & Nakata 2005). Calcium oxalate crystals occur in a wide range of morphologies and can form druses (Figure 6.3). Calcitic spherulites and calcium

oxalate crystals decompose quickly due to micro-organisms (decay of organic matter), but can be visible in burnt or weathered dung remains (Canti 1997; Canti 1999; see also Canti & Brochier 2017a, this book).

# 6.2.1.1 Cattle Dung

Cattle dung is often described as finely layered excre-ment containing relatively long plant fibres (Courty et al. 1989; Macphail et al. 2004; Macphail & Goldberg 2010). Investigations on recent Highland cattle fed on a mixture of different plants (grass, leaves of dicotyledons, etc.) show that the texture of cattle excrements varies depending on fodder (Akeret 2000). Therefore, the excrements are not flat and layered but are rounded in shape (similar to horse dung), relatively loose and the texture is less

regular, similar to the excrements of grazing domestic cattle (Figures 6.1 and 6.2). In fact, various investigations in Neolithic lake shore settlements showed that cattle were fed on leaves, twigs and bark (Akeret 2000; Akeret & Rentzel 2001; Ismail-Meyer 2010; Kuhn et al. 2013). Therefore, especially for prehistoric periods, it must be considered that cattle excrements may vary in shape and texture. The finely layered aspect of cattle dung may be the result of trampling as well as an original characteristic (see also Rentzel et al. 2017, this book). Beside relatively long plant fragments (up to several millimetres long) and a porous (30–50% porosity), partly layered texture, some geogenic silt or sand grains (mineral type according to

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/7b5b89d9a8660a3870cb0f74eaf0a2b57ed2c51e1b71e3ba26675105e4aa6214.jpg)  
Figure 6.3 Highland cattle dung. The plant fragments (mostly stems and leaves) are poorly digested. Note the strong interference colour of the cellulose (C) and some silica phytoliths (P) as well as several calcium oxalate crystals (druses - O). Modern sample. XPL.

the geological setting) can be observed. Those silt and sand grains are usually associated with muddied drinking water or with ingested dust-covered stems and leaves. The same applies to occasionally embedded diatoms (Courty et al. 1989; Shahack-Gross 2011). Furthermore, silica phytoliths, calcium oxalate crystals and dung spherulites can be present in fresh or in well preserved cattle dung (Figure 6.3).

# 6.2.1.2 Sheep/Goat Dung

Sheep coprolites usually are somewhat smaller than cattle and horse excrements and have a rounded shape compared to the smaller (usually  $< 1\mathrm{cm}$ ), slightly elongated, pointed goat pellets (Rasmussen 1993). Both show a regular and smooth surface (Figures 6.4 and 6.5). However, a differentiation between goat and sheep excrements by means of micromorphology is not possible. In contrast to cattle dung, sheep/goat excrements are denser and show a convoluted fabric. The plant fragments are shorter and without any orientation (Courty et al. 1989; Macphail & Goldberg 2010). Besides fragments of stems and leaves, bark, twig wood and spores can be found (Rasmussen 1989, 1993; Akeret & Jacomet 1997; Akeret et al. 1999). The faeces of sheep/goat mainly possess a thin, very dense outer rim ( $< 1\mathrm{mm}$ ), which can show a reddish brown colouring due to oxidation and humification processes (Figure 6.4). In the centre of the coprolites, porosity is significantly higher featuring rounded macropores (Figure 6.4). Digested mineral grains tend to be more frequent and coarser (up to gravel size) than in cattle dung (Figure 6.5). Beside the organic matter, silica phytoliths, calcium oxalate crystals and especially dung spherulites can be abundant (see Canti & Brochier 2017a, this book).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/1efa2509582759c97f9e198ff08c354bf379f78f485d834441c49577f9f6e31d.jpg)  
Figure 6.4 (a) Goat dung pellet. The plant fragments are smaller than those in cattle dung. Note the convolute structure and the  $0.1\mathrm{mm}$  thick, dense, reddish outer rim and the porous inner zone. Modern sample. PPL. Inset: Thin section scan (scale bar length  $0.5\mathrm{cm}$ ). (b) Same as (a), XPL. Note the interference colours of cellulose.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/6317a15997892ebf0c4b12e16b8cd19ec9b742044ff9ea966e9ed1a0b19107e9.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/4fad461c929c48d7391d877ac37ec06fd2f946104c40d75e629500e68a1b0570.jpg)  
Figure 6.5 (a) Experimentally burnt sheep dung pellet. The fire was fed with wood, bones and dried sheep dung and was burning during several hours  $(400 - 800^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$ . Most of the dung turned to ash. Only incomplete burnt pellets could be collected and analysed. Note the convolute structure, the thin outer rim and the porous inner part. One ingested coarse sand grain of local limestone (arrow). Experiment: D. Hager. PPL. Inset: Thin section scan (scale bar length  $0.5\mathrm{cm}$ ). (b) Same as (a), XPL. Note the greyish carbonate matrix in the outer part of the pellet. Inset: The carbonate matrix consists of micritic calcite (POCC; calcitic pseudomorphs after calcium oxalate) and dung spherulites (scale bar length  $0.05\mathrm{mm}$ ).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/250247b9bf6fe20d5a755c25efacf11e68a15ce219cdf57c7953d9c4f7d74908.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/9386d45940ff97c835370b26d68546a86b51cdcec90c94a2bb857cae77dd51b9.jpg)  
Figure 6.6 (a) Fragment of horse dung. The fragments of organic matter vary in size and length and are slightly oriented in places. Modern sample. PPL. (b) Same as Figure (a), XPL. Note the absence of quartz grains.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/4290d99163e7a38d5218ceac0ff0e71f2ceeadacf98a96d682cedf3eab11ed65.jpg)

# 6.2.1.3 Horse/Donkey Dung

In thin section, droppings of horses and donkeys are much more similar to sheep and goat excrements than to those of cattle. Horse/donkey coprolites are denser, showing occasionally a convoluted fabric, which may not always be observed in thin section, but can be observed macroscopically. The size of equid coprolites are much bigger (up to a few centimetres) than those of sheep and goats and usually show a regular and smooth surface. The micromorphological differentiation between the excrements of goat/sheep and horse/donkey is not always evident.

The size of plant remains in the droppings of horse / donkey is usually smaller (mostly up to  $0.5\mathrm{mm}$  - Figures 6.6 and 6.7) than in those of ruminants, owing probably to the different foraging/grazing habits and digestion, horses and donkeys being primarily grazers (Hafez et al. 1969; Loucougaray et al. 2004). In thin section, fragments of organic matter are sometimes slightly oriented but not layered (Figures 6.6 and 6.7). Phytoliths and calcitic dung spherulites may appear (Dejmal et al. 2014); however, calcium oxalate crystals are present in a lower proportion compared with cattle and sheep / goat dung (Canti 1999).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/814760748ceeb9223796e0972976946e9831e0dde3558dc6680bfc690d3474e4.jpg)  
Figure 6.7 (a) Fragment of donkey dung. Note that the organic remains are much shorter and smaller in size than cattle excrements. Modern sample. PPL. (b) Same as (a), XPL. Note the absence of quartz grains.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/38e92d30a4f2ab05b83e29e4e6b0f4f7e9d37a14a1873032f665fc1e771f7f4d.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/dcaecc69257b008230a40bd7bbe085a102beb45c84c0683d4b58c1b9295d41c2.jpg)  
Figure 6.8 (a) Recent house mouse droppings. The elongated excrements show a partly irregular but smooth surface and an irregular structure. They consist mainly of poorly digested seed and stem fragments. Note the  $0.1\mathrm{mm}$  thin dense outer rim. (b) Same as (a), XPL. Note abundant greyish starch grains in two coprolites (arrows). Starch grains are highly soluble and thus usually not preserved in archaeological material.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/0d830a60297f8e97e8cd20d1ba11e1553a0e0eb1cee26405edf1897edd110ea5.jpg)

# 6.2.2 Excrements of Rodents

Some rodent species like the house mouse are intimately connected with human settlements as commensal animals whereas rabbits may have been kept and bred as meat source in certain contexts. It is therefore likely that rodent excrements can be found in archaeological sediments. Nevertheless, only very few micromorphological investigations on rodent excrements have been done until now (Ismail-Meyer 2014). The following descriptions are based on samples from recent rabbits and house mice. For more specific conclusions, more research is advocated.

Recent rabbit droppings appear as rounded pellets (ca.  $1\mathrm{cm}$ ) with an irregular surface without differentiable outer rim. The small, poorly digested plant fragments are

all of the same scale and without any orientation (neither layering nor convolute fabric). Silica phytoliths and cellulose are well preserved, the latter showing its typical interference colours.

Recent excrements of a grain-fed mouse (Rodentia, probably house mouse) have an elongated, pointed shape  $(3 - 4\mathrm{mm}\times 1 - 2\mathrm{mm})$  and a smooth but irregular surface (Figure 6.8). Beyond a thin and dense outer rim, porosity increases significantly. As in rabbit droppings, there is no obvious orientation of the small plant fragments (stems, leaves, seeds, etc.). In recent rodent excrements, phosphatic or amorphous organic groundmass is lacking. Under UV light, only the preserved cellulose is visible. Rabbit and house-mouse excrements may show calcium oxalate crystals, whereas dung spherulites are absent.

# 6.3 Taphonomy

# 6.3.1 Weathering Processes

Postdepositional taphonomic processes are mostly dependent on the environment. In dry (e.g., caves, arid regions) or waterlogged (e.g., lake shore settlements) sediments, organic matter is preserved quite well (Figure 6.9), thus shape and texture of the excrements may still be identifiable (Shahack-Gross 2011). This is especially true for sheep and goat pellets because of their denser, resistant outer rim, whereas cattle dung disintegrates more rapidly. Thus, identifiable fragments of sheep/goat coprolites are more frequent than cattle dung. The colour of weathered faecal remains is usually slightly darker and/or reddish due to immediate humification and oxidation processes. The organic matter can partly turn into an amorphous, brown (PPL) or dark brown (OIL), isotropic organic groundmass (Figure 6.10), especially in waterlogged sediments (Figure 6.11). In the latter, dung spherulites and calcium oxalate crystals are absent and no calcium phosphates (e.g., hydroxyapatite) can be observed (no autofluorescence under UV light). This is particularly the case in dung layers of lakeshore sediments (Ismail-Meyer et al. 2013). The lack of dung spherulites might be due to leaching processes (M. Canti, personal communication).

In moist but not completely anaerobic sediments, the organic material is often replaced by black or reddish iron and/or manganese precipitations forming alteromorphs after former plant remains (Macphail & Goldberg 2010). In well-drained sediments (neither waterlogged nor desiccated environments), on the other

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/a42e5d0b0e2f0ad6aa03476a06c3397fdadd9c2f7f15f92cee8b8855f00bb13c.jpg)  
Figure 6.9 Recent organic rich, layered sheep/goat enclosure sediment. Note slightly weathered (oxidized) vegetable fibres forming a microlaminated structure. Maasai sheep/goat enclosure in Kenya. The enclosure had been abandoned for 20 years. PPL. Image courtesy: R. Shahack-Gross.

hand, organic material is usually not preserved (Shahack-Gross 2011, 2017, this book). Microscopic components like silica phytoliths or dung spherulites can therefore be visible as they are concentrated and not obscured. Thus, weathered ruminant excrements often show accumulations of dung spherulites (Shahack-Gross et al. 2003; Shahack-Gross & Finkelstein 2008; Macphail & Crowther 2008; Matthews 2010; Canti & Brochier 2017a, this book) and/or of laminated silica phytolith strands (Shahack-Gross et al. 2005; Albert et al. 2008;

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/7476a4b695127fe152eae305d2084775f52267077e03fd017d0ed5c5533ac9f1.jpg)  
Figure 6.10 Weathered dung layer, distinguishable as phytolith-rich, microlaminated sediment. Note the wavy levelling of the silica phytolith layers, following the embedded sand grains due to compaction in the course of the degradation of the organic matter. Iron Age settlement of Tel Dor, Israel. PPL. Image courtesy: R. Shahack-Gross.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/b7fcd9f6944460fa04c2fd243ac02bc2a24d68ba1f9095a9d054acaa01937724.jpg)  
Figure 6.11 Fragment of a suspected sheep/goat pellet in waterlogged environment with preserved organic matter and convolute microstructure. Note the reddish brown colour of the organic material and the dense outer rim visible on top. Neolithic lake shore settlement Arbon Bleiche 3, Switzerland. PPL.

Shahack-Gross 2017, this book). However, the quantity of silica phytoliths or dung spherulites in fresh dung may vary considerably, thus a variation in these components has to be expected anyway, regardless of any postdepositional processes.

The decay of organic matter normally leads to an enrichment of phosphate and therefore to phosphate precipitations in or around the excrements (Courty et al. 1989; Karkanas & Goldberg 2010; Macphail & Goldberg 2010; Shahack-Gross 2011, 2017, this book).

# 6.3.2 Firing

Because of the high organic content of herbivore excrements, dried ruminant dung is an excellent fuel, used since prehistoric times and still in use today, mainly in dry regions (Braadbaart et al. 2012; Gur-Arieh et al. 2013). In most cases, burnt dung will be encountered as greyish-white ashy sediment, whereas the shape and fabric of the dung pellets usually is not apparent anymore. The ash consists mainly of more or less intensively transformed biogenic minerals (see also Canti & Brochier 2017b, this book), whereas the organic part burns at temperatures around  $400 - 500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  under oxidizing conditions (Matthews 2010; Shahack-Gross 2011). Dung ash may therefore consist of silica phytoliths (eventually blackened), of dung spherulites and calcitic pseudomorphs after calcium oxalate crystals (POCC; Brochier & Thinon 2003; Shahack-Gross 2011; Canti & Brochier 2017b, this book). The composition of dung ash may vary significantly, since the presence of silica phytoliths, dung spherulites and calcium oxalate crystals depends on the animal's diet as well as on different postdepositional processes. Incompletely burnt excrements may show a mixture of plant fragments and dung spherulites and POCC accumulations (Figures 6.5 and 6.12), when these are not completely destroyed by burning in oxidizing conditions (see Brochier 1996; Brochier 2002; Shahack-Gross 2011; Canti & Brochier 2017a, this book). At high temperatures of approximately  $600 - 900^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , phytoliths will vitrify, producing vesicular phytolith slags (Guelat et al. 1998; Brochier & Claustre 2000; Guelat 2011; Figure 6.13). The transformed phytoliths often are amassed in a sublinear wavy line or can form an amorphous material cementing sand grains together (Thy et al. 1995; Johanson 2004; Braadbaart et al. 2012). Charred dung pellets or fragments thereof consist of black, charred organic material, which usually retain their original shape and texture (Figure 6.14) and result from low firing temperatures or reducing firing conditions. In this case, dung spherulites or POCC may be not observable in thin sections because they are covered with charred organic matter. Furthermore dung spherulites and POCC may disintegrate depending on temperature

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/f52eadc2ff196bbd20bde6ffe77c34f354d16bc4da3d984f7cdfd51258f708ce.jpg)  
Figure 6.12 Experimentally burnt sheep pellet. The resulting ash still preserves the original shape of the pellet and consists mainly of calcitic dung spherulites (S) and of POCC (calcitic pseudomorphs after calcium oxalate - P). XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/6f410ceb5cc75d85c2a3d96fbbace8edf0e1b8cbb4e8b05c7aafbc2818c70e56.jpg)  
Figure 6.13 Burnt cattle stable crust. The sediment mainly consists of layered, mostly melted phytoliths and some ash remains. PPL. Burnt Roman cattle stable, Brig-Glis, Waldmatte, Switzerland. Image courtesy: M. Guelat.

and oxidizing/reducing conditions (Shahack-Gross 2011; see also Canti & Brochier 2017a, this volume).

In the Mediterranean region, burnt dung layers are a common feature in Neolithic and Bronze Age cave and rock shelter sediments (Brochier 1983, 1996; Macphail et al. 1990, 1997; Wattez et al. 1990; Courty et al. 1991; Brochier et al. 1992, 1999; Boschian & Montagnari-Kokelj 2000; Brochier & Claustre 2000; Karkanas 2006; Angelucci et al. 2009; Matthews 2010). The so-called fumiers are characterized by an alternating sequence of thin layers of whitish to grey ash lenses and dark brown to blackish, highly organic, sediments composed of finely fragmented plant tissues. The ruminant excrements were apparently set on fire, so that the organic material was

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/1cd79ae3553be8823a99fa6746b3be4877e505041c63c059eb6cdc48837ed8ea.jpg)  
Figure 6.14 Experimentally charred sheep pellet. The organic material is completely charred. Microstructure is relatively well preserved. Note the still apparent dense outer rim and the large, rounded pores in the centre. Experiment: D. Hager. PPL.

burnt away completely (ashy sediments) or were charred (dark brown to blackish sediments). On the other hand, accidentally burnt stable features are scarce (see Guelat et al. 1998; Rentzel & Guelat 2005). Both preserved stable or enclosure sediments and fumiers allow insights on animal husbandry in the past (see also Shahack-Gross 2017, this volume).

# 6.3.3 Stabling Crusts

Investigations of recent stabling and enclosure sediments reveal well-developed layering and microlamination (Figures 6.13 and 6.14) due to the horizontal alignment of plant fibres (and phytoliths) as consequence of trampling, especially in the stabling of cattle and horses (Shahack-Gross et al. 2003; Macphail et al. 2004; Goldberg & Macphail 2006; Shahack-Gross 2011; Dejmal et al. 2014; Shahack-Gross 2017, this book). Trampled sheep/goat stabling sediments, on the other hand, are composed of compacted pellet fragments, which can show a layering as well (Shahack-Gross et al. 2003; Rentzel et al. 2017, this book). Stabling or enclosure sediments have a lower porosity than equivalent unaffected excrements and show an impact of sediment agglomerates, mineral grains and other components due to trampling processes (Figure 6.15). Such clearly layered and compacted crusts and sediment fragments are found in various archaeological contexts (e.g., Macphail 1994; Guelat et al. 1998; Akeret & Rentzel 2001; Ismail-Meyer & Rentzel 2004; Rentzel & Guelat 2005; Karkanas 2006; Albert et al. 2008; Macphail & Crowther 2008; Shahack-Gross & Finkelstein 2008; Ismail-Meyer 2010; Kuhn et al. 2013; Ismail-Meyer et al. 2013) and may be interpreted as enclosures or stable sediments. In covered stabling,

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/654318dfca4ee5e9b4238e84b63bd5f7c0232c777a3f0ccd1847ff58dd58b77c.jpg)  
Figure 6.15 Fragment of suspected cattle stable/enclosure crust. The upper part is noticeably denser and shows signs of weathering. Cellulose, calcitic inclusions (dung spherulites, calcium oxalate crystals) and calcium phosphates are absent (not distinguishable in PPL). Note the loss of cellular structure of most of the plant fragments. Neolithic lake shore settlement Arbon Bleiche 3, Switzerland. PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/60c8e3337bb210a1b5fb0e7dff32184a1234a7c122228230da92919e2ff12baf.jpg)  
Figure 6.16 Layered horse stable crust. Most of the plant fragments are elongated, reflecting the stabling material or fodder rather than excrements. Medieval stable, Veselí nad Moravou, Czech Republic. PPL.

enrichment with liquid animal waste can form phosphate stained, finely laminated layers with a yellowish to brown amorphous groundmass, which is, due to the presence of calcium phosphate (hydroxyapatite), highly autofluorescent under UV light (Shahack-Gross et al. 2003; Macphail et al. 2004; Macphail & Goldberg 2010; Stoops 2017, this book). Such phosphate-cemented stable sediments and crusts preserve quite well and can be observed in midden deposits or in manured soils (Macphail 2000; Macphail & Goldberg 2010). Macphail et al. (2004) studied a modern horse stable and described

0.9 cm thick crust composed of a laminar organic deposit, which is autofluorescent under blue light suggesting a hydroxyapatite content. The crust is dominated by large (5 mm) monocotyledonous plant fragments. Very little mineral material is present and calcitic spherulites appear to be absent. Crusts from a Medieval horse stable

in Veselí nad Moravou (Dejmal et al. 2014) appeared to be mainly composed of partly decayed organic material consisting of long fragments of grasses (Figure 6.16), embedded in a calcium phosphate-rich, autofluorescent (blue light) cement of hydroxyapatite. Macroscopically, abundant horse hair was visible.

# 7

# Excrements of Omnivores and Carnivores

David Bronnimann<sup>1</sup>, Christine Pümpin<sup>1</sup>, Kristin Ismail-Meyer<sup>1</sup>, Philippe Rentzel<sup>1</sup> and Natalia Éguez<sup>2</sup>

1 IPAS – Integrative Prehistory and Archaeological Science, University of Basel, Switzerland  
$^{2}$  Institute for Pre-and Protohistoric Archaeology, Christian-Albrechts-Universität zu Kiel, Germany

# 7.1 Introduction

The micromorphological analysis of faecal remains started with the investigation of hyena excrements in connection with cave-sediment studies (Goldberg 1980; Horwitz & Goldberg 1989). Since then, micromorphology has become an important method for describing and distinguishing coprolites, and in combination with other methods (microprobe and bulk chemical analysis, palaeoparasitology and pollen analysis, biomolecular analysis, etc.) yielded comprehensive results (Jones 1990; Macphail 2000; Shillito et al. 2011; Shillito & Matthews 2013; Pichler et al. 2014; Sanz et al. 2015, 2016; Macphail 2017; Bronnimann et al. 2017, this book; Pumpin et al. 2017, this book). While carnivore coprolites typically contain bone fragments and an amorphous phosphatic, optically isotropic but highly autofluorescent matrix, omnivore coprolites are much more diverse, depending on specific diets and feeding habits. In fact, identification of human coprolites is difficult because of possible similarities to dog and pig excrements (Courty et al. 1989; Macphail & Goldberg 2010). Additionally, pigs and canids tend to coprophagia (Boes et al. 1997); this can lead to an enrichment of features of different producers, which can falsify the identification of their coprolites by means of micromorphology or intestinal parasite egg and pollen analysis. In summary, identification of the particular producer is not always possible.

# 7.2 Excrements of Omnivores

# 7.2.1 Pig Excrements

Because pig excrements are composed of several semi oval to rounded segments, their texture appears quite irregular. An outer or inner zone cannot be differentiated.

Investigations of recent excrements of wild boar and plant fed miniature pig show that their excrements may consist of numerous plant fragments. The latter are both shorter (mostly  $1\mathrm{mm}$ ) and coarser (thicker) than in cattle or sheep/goat dung and show neither microlamination nor convolute texture (Figures 7.1 and 7.2). The plant tissues are embedded in a reddish brown (PPL) and brown (OIL), optically isotropic (XPL), striated, amorphous organic groundmass (Figure 7.3), which is not autofluorescent in UVF/BLF (no hydroxyapatite) (Macphail & Goldberg 2010). The minerogenic fraction (silt to sand) may be higher than in herbivore excrements and reflects the local geological setting. Beside plant tissues (fragments of stems, seeds, leaves, bark, roots, wood, husks etc.), calcium oxalate crystals are common, whereas spherulites may appear sporadically (Courty et al. 1994; Canti 1997). In addition, intestinal parasite eggs may occur. These can either indicate a proper parasitosis or may derive from ingested excrements (e.g., of humans or herbivores), as coprophagia has been observed in pasture experiments (Boes et al. 1997). In wild boar faeces, thousands of fungal spores can occur (Figure 7.3) (L. Wick, personal communication, 2015). They probably reflect the pigs' characteristic feeding behaviour of grubbing and rooting, as the numerous root fragments in Figure 7.2(a) suggest. Furthermore, this feeding behaviour coincidentally produces heterogeneous soils, as was reported from experimental pig husbandry (Gebhardt 1995, 2007; Macphail & Goldberg 2010).

Another experiment at the 'West Stow Anglo-Saxon Village' showed that pigs provided with a husk-rich cereal feed may produce a laminated, moderately phosphate enriched, trampled surface, whereas some amorphous Fe-P-Ca nodules were formed in the soil immediately below (Macphail & Goldberg 2010; Macphail & Crowther 2011).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/8d3d7df408fe328ab18dd11b8256403d6f0ca1b3342ad5ddde5f052811e25b25.jpg)  
Figure 7.1 (a) Excrement of plant-fed miniature pig, raised on calcareous soil. The organic tissues are embedded in a brown, organic groundmass. There is no regular orientation of the embedded plant fragments. The excrement is highly porous, with irregular and vughy pores. Experimental material, air dried (Burg, Switzerland). PPL. Inset: Thin section scan of whole sample (scale bar length  $0.5\mathrm{cm}$ ). (b) Same as (a), XPL. Note the strong interference colours of cellulose of poorly digested plant remains.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/b7127227d74d3cafc158108618456c86f679bf251ae18108e2fa727f81f1d76b.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/529d6d98ade7fe5ebd598472f3dcb53930268d3d8aa9585bb030b1ddc0205a93.jpg)  
Figure 7.2 (a) Excrement of wild boar. The plant fragments are quite coarse and mainly consist of root fragments (R). Plant tissues are embedded without regular order in a brown to reddish brown, organic groundmass containing numerous quartz and silt grains. Experimental material, air dried (Diessenhofen, Switzerland). PPL. Inset: Thin section scan of whole sample (scale bar length  $1\mathrm{cm}$ ). Sampling: B. Pollmann. (b) Same as (a), XPL. Note the high and strong interference colours of the poorly digested cellulose. The matrix is optically isotropic with the exception of silt and sand grains (mainly quartz).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/6eaa255d3328f9abdee4cf9d17ce7a7206c671474ad2c40ac07a81412feb9e36.jpg)

In an archaeological context, coprolites of domestic pigs (or wild boar) are reported rarely - mostly with uncertain attribution (Macphail 2000; Ismail-Meyer & Rentzel 2004; Ismail-Meyer 2014). This apparent lack of pig excrements in archaeological sediments is probably a consequence of the animals' grubbing activities in themselves, of pig husbandry practices (e.g., herding in forests or in waste lands) and of the remarkable variability of omnivore excrements. Additionally, pig coprolites are likely to dissolve quite quickly due to their porous structure and high amount of fragmented plant tissues.

# 7.2.2 Chicken Droppings

Recent chicken droppings show an unregularly rounded to flattened shape (depending on consistency), whereas the groundmass forms some type of striations (visible in PPL, Figure 7.4) (Karkanas & Goldberg 2010). Within individual striations, the components are more-or-less parallel in orientation. Porosity  $(50 - 60\%)$  is given by numerous, mostly elongated vesicles. Chicken droppings consist of almost every edible item in the surrounding environment. The most common components are small, mostly well preserved plant fragments (usually  $< 0.5\mathrm{mm}$ )

with opal phytolith strands and cellulose with strong interference colours, due to poor digestion of the latter (Figure 7.4). The plant tissues comprise fragments of stems, leaves, seeds, pericarps and roots. They are embedded in a reddish brown (recent sample) to yellowish brown (archaeological sample), amorphous, optically isotropic, nonautofluorescent (recent sample) to autofluorescent (archaeological sample) groundmass with high amount of local sediment (silt to coarse sand grains) (Courty et al. 1994). Furthermore, calcareous earthworm granules, snail and egg shell fragments, calcium oxalate

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/3ed485a86bce4edb9603b2f0ee4457cbb46349c77ca9921a2b19d75b9715668b.jpg)  
Figure 7.3 Excrement of wild boar. The matrix consists of brown amorphous, fine-grained organic material with streaky features. Silt and sand grains from local soil (Q). Note the isolated, well preserved calcium oxalate crystal (Ox) and the large amount of spores (black spots). Experimental material, air dried air dried (Diessenhofen, Switzerland). PPL. Sampling: B. Pollmann.

crystals, and few insect remains can be present occasionally. They reflect the chickens' omnivorous diet. In recent and in very well preserved droppings, grey, finely dotted, elongated striations can be observed, mostly at the irregularly shaped surface or in pores (Figure 7.5). They are built of thousands of avian uric acid spheres, which look somehow similar to faecal spherulites, but show higher interference colours and an irregular, asymmetric shape (Canti 1998; Canti & Brochier 2017, this book) (Figure 7.6). However, avian uric acid are mostly absent in the archaeological record due to their immediate weathering in water (Canti 1998). In archaeological contexts, chicken droppings are rarely identified, probably because of their small size. Some chicken droppings have been observed in medieval backyard sediments in Switzerland (Ismail-Meyer 2010, 2012).

The adaption of birds living around and with humans shows that there is a potential of finding guano in thin section. Especially in caves, bird guano can be quite frequent (e.g., Goldberg & Macphail 2006, 2012; Bergadà et al. 2013). Nevertheless, some bird species also regurgitate pellets after digestion, which may be detected under the microscope. In a suspected Iron Age excarnation structure in Oxley Park (United Kingdom), a large amount of small bones and a high concentration of phosphate was detected, which were interpreted as coprolitic material of scavenging birds (Macphail & Crowther 2008).

# 7.3 Excrements of Carnivores

Carnivore excrements are characterized by a highly phosphatic, cemented, optically isotropic, strongly autofluorescent groundmass with an undifferentiated b-fabric

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/46e167683196224669c973704862079298e6a7998a84d8eafc475d721ad8f955.jpg)  
Figure 7.4 (a) Chicken dropping. Poorly digested plant fragments, silt and sand grains and insect remains (l) are embedded in an amorphous, pale brown, optically isotropic groundmass with streaky appearance. Experimental material, air dried (Bourrignon, Switzerland). PPL. Inset: Thin section scan of dropping sample (scale bar length  $0.5\mathrm{cm}$ ). (b) Same as (a), XPL. Note the interference colours of poorly digested cellulose and the presence of an earthworm granule (E).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/f90c4f4e903d7feef1abd396dd01053b177d0f19b0a0c953810ba1e2fba948c6.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/87bcd5f9fd869094ace409d6d05a52c11b2b9a6db2baaadd779358fbde150e72.jpg)  
Figure 7.5 (a) Suspected chicken dropping (trampled). In the brown, amorphous groundmass (G), a grey, finely dotted, elongated striation can be observed (U). Medieval house floor deposit, dry site, air-dried sample. PPL. (b) Same as (a), XPL. The striation is built of uric acid spheres (U). The phosphatic groundmass (G) is optically isotropic.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/8dbe316896637d1b4061138c9733bb4752e2427790d836a865bf559872d281bc.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/50ba9cda8f298006654dfa5a9e14e5b2b08a7a69f055d01b95a73b40a6a9d11a.jpg)  
Figure 7.6 Detail of Figure 7.5(b) (red box). Avian uric acid spheres look similar to faecal spherulites, but show higher interference colours and an irregular, asymmetric shape. They are only preserved in arid conditions or in roofed areas. XPL.

(Courty et al. 1989; Macphail & Goldberg 2010). The presence of an outer brownish dense cortex, which defines the phosphatic inner matrix has been observed as a common feature in entire carnivore coprolites (Horwitz & Goldberg 1989; Sanz et al. 2016). They can also contain coarse bone fragments, plant tissues, seeds, pseudomorphic voids after decomposed fur or wool as well as mineral grains. Plant remains are observed quite often and probably trace back to incompletely digested bowel content or to coprophagia (Boes et al. 1997; Jones 1990).

# 7.3.1 Hyena Excrements

Hyena excrements are a common feature in cave and rock-shelter sediments (Goldberg 1980; Horwitz & Goldberg 1989; Rodríguez et al. 1995; Carrion et al.

2001; Goldberg et al. 2009; Goldberg & Macphail 2012; Macphail & Goldberg 2012; Sanz et al. 2015, 2016). They consist of several rounded segments with a smooth, regular surface (Horwitz & Goldberg 1989; Larkin et al. 2000). The finely dotted, amorphous groundmass appears yellowish brown to greyish brown under PPL and is optically isotropic, highly autofluorescent in UVF/BLF (Figure 7.7) due to its carbonate hydroxyapatite content (Macphail & Goldberg 2010; Weiner 2010). Dark, mostly dendritic Fe-Mn impregnations are common. A significantly denser and compacted, rich in organic matter, slightly darker outer zone (up to  $1\mathrm{cm}$  thick) related with mineralized intestinal lubricant and desiccation effects can be distinguished from the more porous, paler inner part with numerous vesicular pores. The latter are usually interpreted as deriving from trapped intestinal gases (Horwitz & Goldberg 1989; Sanz et al. 2016). Beside some embedded silt and sand grains (mineralogy corresponding with local geological setting), bone fragments, plant tissues - sometimes with preserved phytoliths - and curved, elongated channels are usually present. The latter are probably pseudomorphic voids after decomposed hair, fur or wool (Horwitz & Goldberg 1989; Rodriguez et al. 1995; Macphail & Goldberg 2012; Sanz et al. 2016). The bone fragments usually show rounded edges as a result of digestion processes (Figure 7.7). Occasionally, there are fragments of ingested excrements (mainly of herbivores) (Horwitz & Goldberg 1989; Larkin et al. 2000), which illustrate the natural behaviour of coprophagia by carnivores.

# 7.3.2 Dog (Canis familiaris) Excrements

Excrements of Canis familiaris are reported regularly in various archaeological contexts (midden and backyard sediments, occupation layers, etc. - Macphail 2000;

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/c6a13650bddbfac63b27d436932ec64415ab9ba4a34f4d659bcbaad2163efffe.jpg)  
Figure 7.7 (a) Suspected hyena coprolite. The amorphous, pale greyish brown groundmass is partly impregnated by finely dotted Fe-Mn precipitations (dark grey). Above, a digested bone fragment with edge rounding is visible (B). Middle Palaeolithic cave sediment, air-dried sample. PPL. Inset: thin section scan (scale bar length  $1\mathrm{cm}$ ). (b) Same as (a), UVF. The amorphous matrix, presumably hydroxyapatite, is highly autofluorescent. The bone fragment (B) appears darker (probably due to digestion processes).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/57bc7e4ed9afa5f3351e20e083ac666989936bfe0ab958488f1a701e2a09c96a.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/b1d7084e9be5c7dbe1f2e1d614de0b00723e47fb350006de31742665cf17e23b.jpg)  
Figure 7.8 Thin section scan of probable dog coprolite. The greyish, highly autofluorescent groundmass (hydroxyapatite) contains numerous bone fragments of several centimetres and few curved voids. Note the dense outer zone of  $1 - 2\mathrm{mm}$ . Identification based on nematode analysis and micromorphology. Iron Age settlement pit infilling, dry site, air-dried sample. PPL.

Ismail-Meyer & Rentzel 2004; Huisman et al. 2009; Macphail & Goldberg 2010, 2012; Huber & Ismail-Meyer 2012; Ismail-Meyer 2012; Ismail-Meyer et al. 2013) but rarely as completely preserved specimens (Figure 7.8).

Excrements of Canis familiaris mostly show an elongated, sometimes segmented shape (Jones 1990). Well-preserved specimens now and then show a small, very dense outer zone (0.5–2 mm thick), whereas the inner part is more porous (porosity depending on diet) and exhibits vesicular to irregular pores (Figure 7.8). In contrast to pig or human coprolites, no obvious texture or orientation of the components can be observed

(neither layering nor striations). Excrements of Canis familiaris are characterized by a brownish grey to grey, speckled, amorphous, optically isotropic and highly autofluorescent groundmass (Courty et al. 1989; Macphail & Goldberg 2010, 2012) (Figures 7.8, 7.9 and 7.10). Mineral grains (silt to sand) occur frequently but are much less common than in pig or chicken excrements. As observed in hyena coprolites, rounded (digested) bone fragments and curved, elongated channels – presumably pseudomorphic voids after hair and fur – are the most common features (Figures 7.8 and 7.9). Additionally, beside organic remains like seed and pericarp fragments or phytolith strands, often associated with reddish to black Fe-Mn precipitations, there are pseudomorphic voids after decomposed plant remains (Figure 7.9) as well as occasional charred vegetal matter crusts and charcoal fragments. The embedded components (plant tissues and bone fragments) are usually several millimetres long and thus clearly bigger than corresponding features in pig or human coprolites.

In waterlogged sediments (e.g., in lakeshore settlements) the groundmass and bone fragments show no autofluorescence in UVF/BLF, which is probably due to leaching processes and dissolution of carbonate hydroxyapatite. For dog coprolites embedded in highly calcareous sediments (e.g., calcareous loess), accumulation of micritic calcareous material (percolation and/or infilling) have been observed (Figure 7.10). Slightly burned carnivore coprolites show reddish to brownish staining, whereas higher temperatures  $(>650 - 900^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$  may lead to calcification and to formation of phytolith slags (melted phytoliths) and vesicular voids (Matthews 2010; Ismail-Meyer et al. 2013).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/2659632d471f79d8a113e80e8347db7b41a328af690b9bd16e2598dec0ca7b17.jpg)  
Figure 7.9 (a) Suspected dog coprolite. Characteristic greyish brown to blackish brown amorphous groundmass with several embedded, rounded bone fragments (B). Some voids (V) probably are pseudomorphs after decomposed plant tissues (cereal remains, seeds etc.), whereas curved, tubular voids (H) are interpreted as negatives of hair or fur. Medieval pit infilling, dry site, air-dried sample. PPL. Inset: Thin section scan (scale bar length  $1\mathrm{cm}$ ). (b) Same as (a), XPL. Note the low interference colours of ingested bone fragments (whitish to grey) with characteristic structure as well as some silt quartz grains, probably ingested with drinking water.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/6cb29dcd7ba4592ad366757557f3969de901f02cacee2e11be739d533c5651fb.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/db1850135f3bb415982dc96928f3dbf87d72c131e357659fed551cdb169dc9de.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/eb4b7cce71b8a5e62c55efbc26c32ba47aec286e1c74328240d3c8504cc22896.jpg)

Figure 7.10 (a) Suspected dog coprolite, partly disintegrated. Pale greyish brown, amorphous groundmass with finely dotted Fe-Mn-impregnation probably due to decomposed organic material. Note ingested charred organic material (C) and some partly curved, elongated channels (suspected negatives of hair and fur – H). Ditch infilling of Neolithic earthwork, dry site, air-dried sample. PPL. Inset: Thin section scan (scale bar length 1 cm). (b) Same as (a), XPL. Some pores and voids are partly filled with micritic calcareous sediment (calcareous loess) in which the coprolite was embedded. (c) Same as (a) under UVF. The amorphous groundmass is highly autofluorescent (hydroxyapatite). The curved, elongated channels are easily recognized (H).

# 7.3.3 Iberian Lynx Excrements

Medium and large felids, such as lynx, are well represented in bone assemblages from Upper Pleistocene karst deposits of Europe (Brugal & Jaubert 1991; Diedrich & Zäk 2006; Marchal et al. 2009; Villa et al. 2010; Fourvel et al. 2015). Analyses on felis carnivore scats are predominantly based on their macromorphology to discriminate between hyaenids or nonhyaenids carnivores (Arribas 1994; Brugal 2010) but some research has been done recently in order to characterize their excrements and internal features at a microscopic scale (Sanz et al. 2016).

Lynx coprolites show an internal microstructure consisting in a fine dotted pale yellow to greyish green phosphatic matrix in PPL, isotropic in XPL, with high

porosity and heterogeneous constituents. These include a few fine, sand-sized quartz grains, some subangular partially phosphatized bone fragments (Figure 7.11a, b) and very common calcitic pseudomorphs associated with hair moulds (Figure 7.11c). Moreover, common voids smaller than those present in hyena (related to digestive gas) and elongated pores (left after decomposed plant remains) can be observed in the matrix along with animal fat (char) and Mn associated dark punctuations (Figure 7.11a). As stated above, lynx excrements have the basic components and internal fabric of carnivore coprolites (Courty et al. 1989; Horwitz & Goldberg 1989) along with a thinner brown external cortex (Figure 7.11d) and a lower degree of bone digestion. This is probably related to the less acidic action of

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/d4e924dbcb0cfe5fbc3ea1c7c86b4bd2018af6e693f651b153f53a2aaecfef2f.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/260f91ad41c9111ac15c7d2e4908dad4c3f7685c9ad0edc9c96233aa334e23e7.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/ebcfd21145df8fd6a45747a80ee7cb1addd64103f3bb05ec0ff998ad104422f5.jpg)  
Figure 7.11 (a) Excrement of modern Iberian lynx (Lynx pardinus). Internal microstructure: note the heterogeneous fine dotted pale yellow to greyish green phosphatic matrix with some subangular partially phosphatized bone fragments and amorphous vegetal remains. Experimental material, air-dried, from ecological reserve (Doñana, Spain). DNA analyses carried out prior to micromorphological study. PPL. (b) Same as (a), XPL. Note the few fine sand-sized quartz grains, elongated pores and the high interference colour of the calcitic hair pseudomorphs. (c) Detail of the calcitic pseudomorphs. All Iberian lynx coprolites that were analysed showed a convoluted pattern on the hair pseudomorphs distribution. Note also the rounded voids related to digested gas in between the pseudomorphs. XPL. (d) Detail of the brownish external cortex. XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/baff592733bf79b30b41a1b352cd349e1319a4c8524ae09a42458d503a7e7974.jpg)

their digestive system because the smaller size of the prey, being essentially wild rabbit (Sanz et al. 2016).

# 7.3.4 Mountain Lion Excrements

Also known as puma or cougar, mountain lion excrements are similar in appearance and size to the Pleistocene now extinct pumas that were inhabiting Africa, Eurasia and America. The excrements internal features show a finely dotted, amorphous groundmass, with a thin brown external cortex, which is pale yellow to greyish green in PPL and isotropic in XPL (Figure 7.12 (a) and (b)) due to its high content in carbonate hydroxyapatite (Macphail & Goldberg 2010; Weiner 2010). Porosity is higher than in lynx excrements although the inner matrix composition and appearance resembles more that of hyena ones due to the sub-rounded cm-sized bone fragments and digestive gases

voids (Figure 7.12c). This possibly derives from the large size of prey, which consists mainly of white-tail deer and eared dove (and occasionally domestic goats when wild prey is scarce). Moreover, some hair calcitic pseudomorphs, seeds and amorphous vegetal remains are also present but in small amounts.

# 7.3.5 Jaguar Excrements

Fossils of extinct Panthera species, such as the European jaguar (Panthera gombaszoegensis) and the American lion (Panthera atrox), show characteristics of both the modern lion and jaguar (Janczewski et al. 1995). Internal microfeatures of jaguar excrements reveal a heterogeneous dotted phosphatized matrix, yellowish to pale grey in PPL and isotropic in XPL (Figure 7.13(a) and (b)). The external brownish cortex is larger and the embedded components like subrounded bone fragments, hair pseudomorphs

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/97b2d3cf6648e0b6e7ec23dd4d2eb5d52f8160905337b66f5228779331bd30c1.jpg)  
Figure 7.13 (a) Excrement of modern jaguar (Panthera onca). Heterogeneous dotted phosphatized matrix with a yellowish to pale grey appearance. Abundant pseudomorphic rounded voids related to digestion gases and elongated pores associated with degraded vegetal tissue are observable along with Fe-Mn impregnations. Note the presence of abundant calcitic grass stem inclusions embedded in the matrix. Experimental material, air-dried, from ecological reserve (Yucatán, México), with DNA analyses carried out prior to micromorphological study. PPL. (b) Detail of digested cm-sized bone. Note the subrounded edges and the internal gas voids. The bone is embedded in a massive phosphatized matrix with some calcitic grass stem inclusions. XPL. (c) Detail of calcitic hair pseudomorphs with the external, organic matter rich, brownish cortex. Some large vegetal inclusions are observable within the cortex along with rounded quartz grains in its outer part, matching the local bio-geological setting. XPL.

and amorphous vegetal tissue are millimetre-sized (Figure 7.13b, c). Especially notable is the presence of calcitic grass stems (Figure 7.13b). Their abundance can be related with incompletely digested bowel content from the prey (collared peccaries and capybaras mostly) but also with zoopharmacognosy (the behaviour of eating grass to induce regurgitation of undigested matter, as fur or unguis from prey or self-grooming - Sanz et al. 2016). Abundant pseudomorphic rounded voids related to digestion gases and elongated pores associated with degraded vegetal tissue are also observable, along with Fe-Mn impregnations (Figure 7.13a).

# 7.4 Human Excrements

Because of great diversity of human diets, a general description of human excrements proves difficult, especially because well preserved excrement remains seem rare in archaeological contexts. Entirely preserved coprolites are known from exceptional circumstances like mummy intestinal remains, or from pelvic areas in burials (Macphail et al. 2013; Usai et al. 2014). More often, small fragments of supposed human coprolites are reported in different archaeological features like (transported) manured soils (Henning & Macphail 2004), midden

sediments (Macphail et al. 1990; Macphail 1994, 2000; Shillito et al. 2011; Shillito & Matthews 2013), organic rich infillings of settlement pits (Rentzel 1998; Pichler et al. 2014) or occupation layers (Rentzel 1997; Ismail-Meyer & Rentzel 2004). Due to the strong fragmentation, reliable identification of human coprolites by means of micromorphology appears problematic. Better preserved human faeces are found in cesspit and latrine sediments (Hermann 1985; Rentzel 1999; Pumpin 2013). In order to securely identify human coprolites, it is necessary to combine micromorphology with other methods like the study of endoparasite eggs (Courty et al. 1989; Jones 1990; Macphail 2000; Bouchet et al. 2003; Macphail & Goldberg 2010; Pumpin et al. 2017, this book), biomolecular analysis (analysis of sterol faecal biomarkers and bile acid faecal biomarkers) as well as gas chromatography (GC) and mass spectroscopy (MS) (Shillito et al. 2011; Shillito & Matthews 2013; Sistiaga et al. 2014a, 2014b; Shillito 2017, this book).

Human coprolites usually have an amorphous, yellowish brown (PPL) and optically isotropic, strongly autofluorescent groundmass (carbonate hydroxyapatite matrix), which can appear reddish to orange under OIL (Macphail 2000; Macphail & Goldberg 2010; Shillito et al. 2011) (Figure 7.14a). Sometimes, reddish to brown-blackish, dendritic striations and Fe-Mn impregnations occur, presumably due to decayed organic material. In contrast to dog coprolites, the groundmass can form striations in which embedded components and Fe-Mn impregnations run in parallel (Figure 7.14a). Thus, an unarticulated orientation and a streaky structure become evident. No outer or inner zone can be distinguished. Porosity depends on diet and preservation: excrements with

pseudomorphic voids after decomposed plant remains are strongly porous (35–40% porosity), whereas well-preserved or bone-rich excrements may be much denser (10–15% porosity). Pore shape can be linear and elongated (moldic voids) as well as rounded to vesicular (the latter presumable due to trapped gases – Figure 7.14).

Due to the varied diet, human coprolites can contain different kinds of plant tissue like fragments of seeds, legume testa, cereal or vegetable remains. Phytolith strands may be visible, whereas cellulose shows strong interference colours (Macphail & Goldberg 2010). Rounded bone fragments and fish scales may be present, although they are rarer and smaller than in dog coprolites. Additionally, intestinal parasite eggs, fungal spores and few mineral grains (silt to sand) occur, the latter being scarce compared to pig and dog excrements. In general, ingested components (plant remains, bones etc.) seem to be smaller than in dog faeces (mostly  $0.5 - 1\mathrm{mm}$ , sometimes  $2 - 3\mathrm{mm}$ ).

Bioturbation and weathering of human coprolites lead to an amorphous, undifferentiated structure (Shillito et al. 2011). Human excrements embedded in waterlogged sediments lose their autofluorescence, probably due to leaching processes.

In latrines and cesspits, there are yellowish-brown faecal layers (Herrmann 1985; Rentzel 1999; McCobb et al. 2001; Marshall et al. 2008; Macphail 2017) (Figure 7.15), sometimes containing mineralized plant remains or moldic voids of the same as well as ingested bone fragments, intestinal parasite eggs (Herrmann 1985) and phosphatic pseudomorphs of decomposed plant remains (McCobb et al. 2001; Marshall et al. 2008; Pümpin 2013).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/740fd9284c3499fc2b31a1813e11120f2027de3a5628e7616af5a6b76aaeb07e.jpg)  
(a)
