Figure 7.14 (a) Suspected human coprolite. The reddish brown amorphous groundmass is optically isotropic and nonautofluorescent, probably as a result of leaching processes. Some striations are recognizable. Some of the curved and elongated voids probably represent pseudomorphs of decomposed plant tissues. Roman latrine infilling, waterlogged site, air-dried sample. PPL. Inset: Detail of thin section scan (scale bar length  $1\mathrm{cm}$ ). (b) Same sample as (a). Note the numerous half-decomposed to completely decomposed plant tissues (D), leaving behind elongated and angular pores. Note the single quartz sand grain (Q). PPL.  
(b)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/bbb35fed72fc9d459f4f343b6c6d5bc00f116ba650a2fe6d993631ce1dcaa54d.jpg)  
Figure 7.15 (a) Yellowish brown to pale greyish brown, highly phosphatic, autofluorescent faecal layer (C) in a Medieval cess pit (dry site, air-dried sample), probably developed because of intrusion of liquid faecal waste or surface water. Above the lower layer, greyish ashy material can be observed. Thin section scan. PPL. (b) Detail of (a) (red box). The middle part (pale grey with embedded charcoal fragments and numerous silt and sand grains) is a faecal layer (devoid of coprolites stricto sensu). It contains highly phosphatic, silty sediments. Beneath and above, some small coprolite fragments (Co) are visible. PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/f56a8cbcf5b642eed74f5aff96a50cdab4009c88df2aa3c46a53f344872fa5c7.jpg)

Because of percolating water, pale yellow crusts and Fe-Ca-phosphate nodules are frequent (Rentzel 1999; Macphail & Goldberg 2010; Pümpin 2013), sometimes with neoformations of vivianite crystals (Karkanas & Goldberg 2010). This mineralized cess is usually highly autofluorescent (presumably due to carbonate hydroxyapatite – Macphail & Goldberg 2010) and optically isotropic. Faecal layers in latrines can be interfingered with ashy sediments and/or charcoal, probably for hygienic reasons (Brombacher et al. 2000; Deforce 2010). Corresponding material can be found in fields, which is a sign that latrine and cesspit sediments were used as 'night soil' fertilizer (Henning & Macphail 2004; Goldberg & Macphail 2006; Macphail & Goldberg 2010).

# 7.5 Future Research

The study of animal and human excrements is an important aspect in micromorphological investigations of archaeological sediments. Experimental work, as at the Ancient Butser Farm experiment (Macphail et al. 2004), has provided essential information about animal husbandry and resulting faecal sediments. Also central for excrement investigations are collections of (recent) excrement samples with information about the producers' diets. Depending on the archaeological context, the presence of different coprolites species may differ largely. Human and carnivore interaction during the Pleistocene has become one of the main concerns in the study of ancient hominid behaviour, therefore for Palaeolithic research an extensively and deep investigation in micromorphology and

taphonomy should be carried out, characterizing wild Felis and Canis species from different actual ecological niches. Different research questions rise for the Neolithic and later periods: while cattle and sheep/goat dung is recognized frequently in archaeological sediments, more emphasis should be employed in omnivores – especially pig, human and different species of Canidae – due to the need to accurately identify husbandry and domestication processes. While the effects of trampling, firing and weathering on herbivore excrements is quite well understood (Bronnimann et al. 2017, this book; Canti & Brochier 2017, this book; Rentzel et al. 2017, this book), there is only a little information about those processes on carnivore and omnivore coprolites. Further points to keep in mind are the health status as well as the daily dietary of the individual, affecting significantly the appearance of the excrements (see Lewis & Heaton 1997).

Nevertheless, the presence of an amorphous, phosphatic groundmass, as in omnivore and carnivore faeces, allows them to be differentiated, in a first step, from herbivore excrements, the latter comprising a high content of plant remains of different size (Bronnimann et al. 2017, this book). A second step, analysing the shape and structure of the coprolite itself, the content of digested materials, their distribution within the coprolite as well as shape and composition of the voids, narrows down the determination of the producer. However, the determination is not as simple as described, particularly when diagnostic inclusions are absent, when the excrements are heavily weathered or only present in small (phosphatic) fragments (e.g., in middens or 'dark earth'). Often the origin of phosphate aggregates in sediment is difficult to

recognize and its attribution to coprolites remains open. In this case the combination of micromorphology with other procedures such as microprobe scanning electron microscopy with energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (SEM-EDAX), Fourier transformed infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) and loss on ignition analyses could help to characterize complementary geochemical information (Sanz et al. 2015, 2016) and is a promising approach for further research. Not only may the analyses of intestinal parasite eggs embedded in the matrix refer to the origin of the producer (Pumpin et al. 2017, this book) – another possibility is the biomolecular analysis of organic residues by GC-MS. This method, measuring biochemical markers such as sterols and bile acids, enables the distinction between faecal and nonfaecal products and permits the identification of omnivore and ruminant excrements as well as omnivore species (Shillito et al. 2011; Sistiaga et al. 2014a, 2014b; Shillito 2017, this book).

Combining all the different methods allows for a better recognition and identification of coprolites including the taphonomic processes affecting them. This enables us to gain a better understanding of the use of space in and around archaeological settlements. In addition, specific behaviours can be recognized like composting latrine and cess sediments or using them as fertilizer. Once exposed to aerobic conditions, bioturbation brings about a reworking of the material, inducing postdepositional change of the faeces. For identifying faecal features and differentiating these from phosphatic aggregates (nodules, crusts), the most promising way in micromorphology is to do more experimentation, especially to gain information about weathering and firing processes. Thus, more experimental and interdisciplinary studies will help to increase our knowledge about excrements and postdepositional processes in the archaeological record.

Shillito, L.-M. & Matthews, W. (2013) Geoarchaeological investigations of midden formation processes in the

early to late Ceramic Neolithic levels at Catalhöyuk, Turkey ca. 8550-8370 cal BP. Geoarchaeology 28, 25-49.

Villa, P., Sánchez-Goni, M. F., Cuenca-Bescós, G. et al. (2010) The archaeology and paleoenvironment of an Upper Pleistocene hyena den: an integrated approach. Journal of Archaeological Science 37, 919-935.

8

# Guano

Panagiotis Karkanas

The Malcolm H. Wiener Laboratory for Archaeological Science, American School of Classical Studies, Athens, Greece

# 8.1 Introduction

Guano is a byproduct of bat, bird and pinnipeds (seals in general) excretion and decomposition. Guano deposition is believed to be the most important agent of organic input in caves, some insular sediment and ornithogenic soils (Wilson & Bain 1976; Karkanas et al. 2000, 2002; Shahack-Gross et al. 2004; Simas et al. 2007; Schaefer et al. 2008; Pereira et al. 2013). So far, archaeological sites associated with insular sediments have not been reported, although archaeological soils have been described recently under ornithogenesis in Antarctica (Villagran et al. 2013). As a primary component of archaeological sites, therefore, guano is found predominantly in caves. Guano has an exceptionally high content of nitrogen, phosphate and potassium, so it has been used widely as manure. However, only bird guano has reportedly been used as manure in antiquity, although direct evidence of its use is lacking (Szpak et al. 2012). This is in contrast with dung, which was probably used from the Neolithic (Bogaard et al. 2013). Composition of guano varies with the diet of the type of animal and its feeding habits and this affects decomposition products, microstructure and morphological characteristics (Shahack-Gross et al. 2004; Emerson & Roark 2007).

# 8.2 Micromorphology

The characteristic microscopic appearance of fresh guano is a bedded spongy or aggregated microstructure and vughy porosity (Figures 8.1a, 8.1b and 8.2a) (Hutchinson 1950; Cullen 1988; Shahack-Gross et al. 2004; Karkanas & Goldberg 2010a; Bergada et al. 2013). Types of guano particularly enriched in plant organic remains, like fruit-bat guano, consist of dark to black fibrous fragments of amorphous organic material (Figure 8.1b). Most other types of guano contain various coarse inclusions, such as

dissolved fine bone fragments, insect scales, chitin and plant remains, depending on the diet of the animal (Figure 8.2a). Bird guano may consist of dark yellowish cryptocrystalline apatite (undifferentiated b-fabric). Microcrystalline struvite  $(\mathrm{NH}_4)\mathrm{MgPO}_4\cdot 6\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O})$  can also be a major constituent of some fresh bird (gull) guano (Cullen 1988). In contrast to bird guano, fresh bat guano does not contain apatite. All types of guano are particularly prone to bioturbation resulting thus in large amounts of organic excrements (Figures 8.3a and 8.3b).

In archaeological sites, fresh guano is unlikely to be preserved; its alteration products are therefore the only indication of its past presence. One of the first alteration products of guano is gypsum (Shahack-Gross et al. 2004). It occurs as nodules and void infillings with a xenotopic fabric but also as dispersed euhedral to subeuhedral crystals, often associated with guano-derived organic remains (Figures 8.1b and 8.3b). Similar gypsum features, though, have been observed in dung (Brochier et al. 1992; Shahack-Gross 2011). The phosphate minerals ardealite  $\mathrm{(Ca_2(HPO_4)(SO_4)\cdot 4H_2O)}$ , brushite  $\mathrm{(Ca(PO_3OH)\cdot 2H_2O)}$ , struvite, and newberyite  $\mathrm{(Mg(PO_3OH)\cdot 3H_2O)}$  have also been reported in guano deposits (Karkanas et al. 2002; Shahack-Gross et al. 2004; Karkanas & Goldberg 2010a; Ajas et al. 2013) but they are rarely preserved in the sediment or soil records. Newberyite, in particular, has been found in altered cave sediments from archaeological sites (Karkanas et al. 2002); it has an acicular crystal habit and occurs as rosette-like aggregates.

Alteration of guano almost always produces apatite (Courty et al. 1989; Macphail & Goldberg 1999; Karkanas et al. 2000; Shahack-Gross et al. 2004; Ajas et al. 2013). In all types of guano deposits, apatite is microcrystalline. Apatite occurs as groundmass material, or infillings and coatings of voids (Figures 8.4a and 8.4b). Phosphate pedofeatures associated with decayed guano occurrences in archaeological sites include light yellow, cryptocrystalline typic apatite nodules. In limestone and other coarse calcareous

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/d50fd9c9e2cec200efd9d3ac500cc2d0a4db357378aa73251b2e862c0431ef2e.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/7b8c8af1b295fbb587d1cc84879d404325bd1766f6c325486ea08b6bcbd06550.jpg)  
Figure 8.1 Sample from a modern heap of relatively fresh bat guano (Adritsaina Cave, Greece): (a) Thin section scan showing organic matter with an open bedded fabric and a vughy to spongy porosity. (b) Detail showing fibrous fragments of amorphous organic matter with nodules of gypsum with xenotopic fabric. XPL.

fragments, apatite is often found as yellowish hypocoatings (reaction rims: Figures 8.5a and 8.5b). Often, however, cryptocrystalline apatite and other phosphates are pseudomorphic after guano microstructures and appear in the form of fibrous features (Figures 8.2b and 8.6). Nevertheless, neoformed (authigenic) apatite alone is not a direct indication of guano presence as dung alteration can also produce apatite (Bertran & Raynal 1991; Macphail 2000; Karkanas and Goldberg 2010a; Macphail & Goldberg 2010). Phosphatized dung, though, has often a more massive, crustlike appearance, but nodular features are reported in anthropic sediments associated with animal dung and human waste (Macphail et al. 2004; Macphail & Goldberg 2010).

Although apatite and other phosphate minerals can be recognized in thin sections by their microcrystalline, amorphous appearance (Figures 8.7a, 8.7b, 8.8a and 8.8b), autoflorescence in blue light is an additional feature indicating their presence (Figure 8.4b) (Altemüller & Van Vliet-Lanoë 1990; Macphail & Goldberg 1999; Goldberg & Macphail 2006). However, only with the use of additional mineralogical identification techniques (e.g., XRD, FTIR, EDS) is it possible to identify specific phosphate minerals (Goldberg & Nathan 1975; Karkanas et al. 1999, 2000; Macphail & Goldberg 2000). Additional phosphatic pedofeatures in cave environments related to the decay of guano include a suite of aluminum-phosphate minerals such as taranakite  $\mathrm{(K_3Al_5(PO_3OH)_6(PO_4)_2\cdot 18H_2O)}$ , crandellite  $\mathrm{(CaAl_3(PO_4)}$ $\mathrm{(PO_3OH)(OH))}$ , variscite  $\mathrm{(AlPO_4\cdot 2H_2O)}$ , montogomeryite  $\mathrm{(Ca_4MgAl_4(PO_4)_6(OH)_4\cdot 12H_2O)}$  and leucophosphite

Figure 8.2 (a) Fresh collared dove excrement with chitin (Ch) remains and a fibrous vughy fabric. PPL. (b) Decayed guano with an orange phosphate nodule (Ph). ![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/7f609f88ba3df07a4107894c635243a9ecb2739651d9ab6548824da3dcbf7521.jpg)

Figure 8.5 Apatite (Ap) hypocoating on a calcareous shell. Note the yellowish alteration rim in PPL (a) and its isotropic appearance in XPL (b) in the areas where the alteration to apatite is complete (PP13B Cave, Pinnacle Point, S. Africa).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/02161b99748216484c6bcdf0bb02850c270f7292735b30c4f7f3d30c640f01bb.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/da09ae7f7b1c9df1c115bb473d147727d5b5abbe6dcc053650adf6cc682160c4.jpg)  
Figure 8.6 Phosphatized guano with characteristic open-bedded fibrous fabric (PP13B Cave, Pinnacle Point, S. Africa). PPL.

$(\mathrm{K}(\mathrm{Fe}^{3+})_2(\mathrm{PO}_4)_2(\mathrm{OH})\cdot 2\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O})$ . They occur in the form of microcrystalline nodules and infillings of platy and lenticular voids, formed in sediments affected by freezethaw activity in Palaeolithic caves (Karkanas 2001), as coatings and hypocoatings and as nodules, often with a banded, wavy, or geodic internal fabric (Figures 8.7a, 8.7b, 8.8a and 8.8b) (Karkanas & Goldberg 2010a). Their color varies mostly from yellow to orange but white, grey and brown hues are also observed.

# 8.3 Archaeological Implications

Identification of the presence of past guano deposits has several implications for the archaeological record. In caves, massive guano deposits related to intensive bat roosting are regarded as a general indication of human

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/44e1c4b61379fd4757a74d6a2366a6ddea96579abf6c8ea9fc8466b5931771f8.jpg)  
Figure 8.7 Light and dark brown montgoryite nodules in burnt remains altered by degrading guano (Klissoura Cave, Greece). (a) PPL; (b) XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/b8106a5dc48f247d6f83f2858b5542c7a13eea18726f756a04a69daaf4f2f508.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/141f79e0f8637e5a5a06ec09fec0ae64e4b8f230db3ec5a1c416a70202b85fe2.jpg)  
Figure 8.8 Taranakite aggregated nodule (Tr) surrounded by leucophosphite spherulites (L) in burnt sediment altered by degrading guano (Klissoura Cave, Greece). (a) PPL; (b) XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/f8d9d1e3affb4bada04062a480e6be12f24d65196fb9d570cd9b8562c0fc5e9f.jpg)

absence (Courty et al. 1989; Karkanas et al. 2000; Bergada et al. 2013). The study of the alteration products of guano has provided invaluable information on the alteration of materials of primary archaeological interest such as bone, ash and shell. Degradation of guano produces an acidic environment and releases phosphorous in the pore water (Shahack-Gross et al. 2004). Under these conditions, all calcareous materials and eventually bones are no longer stable, and may dissolve. Impressive sequences of phosphatized ash layers have been attributed to alteration processes related to decomposing guano (Karkanas et al. 2000; Meignen et al. 2007; Weiner et al. 2007). Bone and shell distribution in archaeological caves is often governed by guano-related diagenetic processes and is not the product of human behavior alone (Schiegl et al. 1996; Karkanas et al. 1999; Weiner et al. 2002; Karkanas & Goldberg 2010b; Karkanas 2010).

Furthermore, guano-related alteration features can provide valuable stratigraphic information. The presence of recognizable guano crusts and other guano deposits indicates stable surface exposure with lack of sedimentation. Black crusts consisting of dark reddish yellow cryptocrystalline apatite are often formed in cave environments and mark periods of stabilization and exposure of the sediment on the surface (Karkanas et al. 2008; Karkanas & Goldberg 2010b; Bergada et al. 2013).

# 8.4 Future Prospects

The study of guano and its alteration products has important implications for the study of the archaeological record and particular for the diagenetic effects on several material of interest. The micromorphology of fresh bat guano and its phosphate decomposition products are relatively well known. However, there are other reactions, which involve bacterial decomposition of guano and the formation of iron, manganese and sulfur minerals (Hill & Forti 1997). These processes are essentially unknown and their mineral identification might need advanced instrumental techniques (e.g., Mossbauer analysis and magnetic studies). Micromorphology will reveal the textural relationships, degree of alteration of the minerals and the sequence of mineral development.

Moreover, there are not many similar studies on the diagenesis of bird guano. Several phosphatic features in cave environments are only tentatively related to guano decomposition products because most of the features produced by the alteration of bird guano are unknown. Future studies should focus on studying such features, although the vast amounts of different bird species nesting in caves makes this endeavor quite difficult.

# 9

# Ova of Intestinal Parasites

Christine Pümpin<sup>1</sup>, Matthieu Le Bailly<sup>2</sup> and Sandra Pichler<sup>1</sup>

$^{1}$  IPAS – Integrative Prehistory and Archaeological Science, University of Basel, Switzerland  
$^{2}$  University of Bourgogne Franche-Comte, CNRS UMR Chrono-environment, Besançon Cedex, France

# 9.1 Introduction

Even though coprolites or palaeofaeces are the primary source of palaeoparasitological evidence, parasite eggs occur in a whole range of archaeological contexts. Latrines and stable deposits but also occupation layers, pits and middens, caves and mines have yielded parasite remains (see Cockburn et al. 1998; Jouy-Avantin et al. 1999; Aspöck et al. 2000; Bouchet et al. 2003; Loreille & Bouchet 2003). Micromorphology can be used to identify parasite remains (primarily eggs) occurring in coprolites and sediments from archaeological contexts. A number of parasites have already been detected in micromorphological thin sections, such as Trichuris sp., Capillaria sp. and Ascaris sp. remains, particularly in the Roman vicus of Kempraten, Switzerland (Pümpin 2013), or Capillaria eggs in the Neolithic lake site of Arbon Bleiche 3, Switzerland. Ascaris eggs have also been identified in Late Iron Age and Medieval samples from Basel, Switzerland (Rentzel 1998; Brombacher et al. 1999; Pichler et al. 2014).

# 9.2 Characteristics of Specific Parasite Eggs

There are several thousand species of helminths comprising the classes of cestodes, nematodes and trematodes, which live inside vertebrates (Hugot et al. 2001). In modern central Europe about 30 species seriously affect humans (Aspöck & Walochnik 2007) and many more infest animals. In thin sections of archaeological sediments, eggs originating from coprolites bear witness to ancient endoparasitoses.

Here only the most frequently encountered and characteristic types of helminth eggs found in thin sections

of archaeological sediments from Central Europe are presented: whipworm (Trichuris sp.), hairworm (Capillaria sp.) and roundworm (Ascaris sp.). Tapeworm eggs (Taenia sp.) are included because of their frequent occurrence in palaeoparasitological investigations and to prevent misidentification. Even with classical flotation, the identification of parasite eggs is not always straightforward because of strong morphological similarities between species and variable egg morphometrics within the same species (Thienpont et al. 1990; Moravec 1994; Bouchet et al. 2003). The colour of parasite eggs in micromorphological thin sections is evaluated by observation in PPL and XPL. Due to diverse taphonomic processes, colour may vary, however. In the present study, eggs from mineralized coprolites rich in phosphatic matrix of dry sediments as well as eggs found in phosphatic matrix of waterlogged sediments are presented.

# 9.2.1 Trichuris sp. (Whipworm)

Whipworms belong to the Nematoda phylum. The Trichuridae family comprises, among others, the genera Trichuris and Capillaria (see below). There are over 70 species of Trichuris (Goncalves et al. 2003) infesting vertebrates like humans, swine, cattle, sheep and canids, with all eggs being identical in morphology (Thienpont et al. 1990; Le Bailly 2005). Mature eggs are ingested by dirt contamination (e.g., by consumption of insufficiently cleaned food items or poor hygiene). This endoparasite is found worldwide (Boch & Supperer 1977; Hahn et al. 2009; Hof & Dörries 2009).

- Morphology: barrel or lemon shaped with bipolar plugs (Figure 9.1).  
- Morphometric: T. trichuria (Homo), T. suis (Sus)  $50 - 68\mu \mathrm{m}$  (length)  $\times 21 - 31\mu \mathrm{m}$  (width); eggs of species affecting rodents, cattle, sheep and canids from 67-90 (length)  $\times 30 - 42\mu \mathrm{m}$  (width).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/e8bd49a54b67f437d5bf69edeb7f3e8482338a85d32093e719950fd7447fc5a4.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/27e748549aa34f06e660c0878187b62793783bf499a4753430439c145b060bdd.jpg)  
Figure 9.1 Human coprolite with a Trichuris egg. Clearly visible are the two bipolar plugs on top and on the bottom of the lemon-shaped body. Waterlogged Roman latrine, Kempraten, Switzerland. (a) PPL; (b) XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/835260dd6cb67dd9d5666a77118a512c746e75b5867c71ca2c5e0b208ba17714.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/98e76d33577cee2e82d3c578318c70371686caacc780b5666a510d2abf60722d.jpg)  
Figure 9.2 Latrine infill enclosing Capillaria eggs. Their shape is very similar to that of whipworm eggs, with the exception of the surface structure being rough and ornamented. The Capillaria egg belongs to the group with dense and deep punctuated pattern. Waterlogged Roman latrine, Kempraten, Switzerland. (a) PPL; (b) XPL.

- Shell: thick, highly resistant to soil taphonomic processes.  
- Surface structure of shell: smooth and without ornamentation.  
Core: mostly missing.  
Colour observed in waterlogged sediment: brown to dark yellow (PPL)/dark yellow (XPL).  
Colour observed in dry sediment: brown to yellow (PPL)/dark yellow (XPL).

# 9.2.2 Capillaria sp. (Hairworm)

Hairworms are widespread cosmopolitan nematodes infesting primarily rats and mice. In rare cases, humans and other mammals can also be affected. Infection of a suitable host is achieved by contact with infected soil

and ensuing consumption of the eggs (Ferreira & Andrade 1993; Lucius & Loos-Frank 2008). Capillaria eggs are very similar in shape to those of Trichuris but they can be distinguished by their characteristic surface structures (Le Bailly 2005; Fugassa et al. 2008; Le Bailly et al. 2014).

- Morphology: barrel shaped with bipolar plugs (Figures 9.2 and 9.3).  
- Morphometric: Capillaria hepatica and C. aerophila (rodents, humans, cats, dogs)  $48 - 74\mu \mathrm{m}$  (length)  $\times$ $29 - 40\mu \mathrm{m}$  (width); eggs of species affecting birds  $43 - 65\mu \mathrm{m}$  (length)  $\times$ $20 - 35\mu \mathrm{m}$  (width); eggs of species affecting fish  $60 - 78\mu \mathrm{m}$  (length)  $\times$ $27 - 39\mu \mathrm{m}$  (width).  
- Shell: thick, highly resistant to soil taphonomic processes.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/803e46e4d4d4023f39288670db9d49ea09aef950dd46ebbd792cb7475f4c4dc5.jpg)  
Figure 9.3 This Capillaria egg belongs to the group with a dense net pattern. Waterlogged Roman latrine, Kempraten, Switzerland. (a) PPL; (b) XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/c0de858a1a83954036b59f2d3b82d3a8d73bb8f81f397940361ad112c5a0cd74.jpg)

- Surface structure of shell: rough and ornamented. Some, like Capillaria hepatica and C. aerophila show a more-or-less dense reticulate surface structure. Others, like Capillaria brevispicula and C. tuberculata show surfaces with punctuated patterns varying in density and depth.  
- Colour observed in waterlogged sediments: brown to dark brown (PPL) / dark yellow (XPL).  
Colour of eggs in dry sediments and core feature not observed.

# 9.2.3 Ascaris sp. (Roundworm)

Roundworms belong to the Nematoda. Especially the Ascaris sp. group of the Ascarididae family are cosmopolitan parasites, and can be found in humans and swine worldwide (Aspöck & Walochnik 2007; Hahn et al. 2009). Ingestion of mature eggs occurs by dirt contamination (e.g., poor hygiene, infected soil or consumption of poorly or unwashed vegetables treated with manure). Eggs of A. lumbricoides (Homo) and A. suum (Sus) are identical in morphology and physiology (Gonçalves et al. 2003; Loreille & Bouchet 2003) but vary between larger unfertilized and smaller fertile ones (Thienpont et al. 1990).

- Morphology: ellipsoid (Fig. 9.4).  
- Morphometric: Unfertilized eggs  $88 - 94\mu \mathrm{m}$  (length)  $\times$ $39 - 44\mu \mathrm{m}$  (width); fertile eggs  $45 - 75\mu \mathrm{m}$  (length)  $\times$ $35 - 60\mu \mathrm{m}$  (width).  
- Shell: thick, mostly resistant to soil taphonomic processes.  
- Surface structure of shell: mamillated, undulated layer.  
- Core: colourless and structured (can be granulous, striated or may be missing).

- Colour observed in waterlogged sediment: brown to dark yellow (PPL)/grey or dark yellow (XPL).  
- Colour observed in dry sediment: clear brown to yellow to colourless (PPL) / dark yellow and in some cases isotropic (XPL).

# 9.2.4 Taenia sp. (Tapeworm)

Tapeworms belong to the Cestoda, which are subdivided into different families. Only the Taeniidae, especially Taenia sp., are described below. Around 45 species (Lucius & Loos-Frank 2008) are known, for which, among others, humans and canids are the final hosts. By consuming contaminated raw or undercooked beef or pork, the infection is transmitted to humans. The eggs of all Taenia species are very similar in size and nearly indistinguishable when recovered from samples of unknown biological origin (Bouchet et al. 2003). Although Taenia has not yet been observed in micromorphological thin sections, we present the eggs to prevent misidentification.

- Morphology: spherical (Figure 9.5).  
- Morphometric: embryoophores  $31 - 40\mu \mathrm{m}$  in diameter.  
- Outer shell: Taenia sp. eggs have a fragile and flat outer hyaline membrane. In many cases the membrane does not resist the stress of expulsion and even less so soil taphonomic processes (Le Bailly 2005). As a rule, only embryophores are present, which exhibit a characteristic inner shell.  
- Surface structure of embryophore shell: the shell is thick and has a characteristic radial striation.  
Colour: brown (PPL). Observation under XPL is missing.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/592d703ec3675b2945651efb6ee7503eb6c7fe8af74e1cbab09cf9f8e83d90ff.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/5cd6c35b850ffe5e85eaf7d3911e460d17f16a95a118b093af68b9880fcf94b7.jpg)  
Figure 9.4 Mineralized, phosphatic coprolite of human infected by Ascaris lumbricoides. Note the translucency of the egg and the striated core. Medieval latrine, Bäumleingasse, Basel, Switzerland. (a) PPL; (b) XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/0802be94bdb57b3c0dbec505cbcc8a8053e6f681cc8749dde6d157dd16f6f298.jpg)  
Figure 9.5 Modern Taenia egg  $(\varnothing = 34\mu \mathrm{m})$  from Entzheim, France. Standard, rehydrated and micro-sieved samples. PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/8b3d3641e790cc9a44d2ac07100090b96d1678b5c98df6e674db07c4b0b0c43f.jpg)  
Figure 9.6 From left to right: Amphitrema flavum (testate amoeba); Diporotheca rhizophila; Arnium sp. and Persiciospora sp. The shape of Amphitrema and the fungal spores is similar to Trichuris sp. and can easily be confused. Image courtesy: B. van Geel (see van Geel et al. 2003; van Geel & Aptroot, A. 2006).

# 9.3 Limitations of the Study of Helminth Eggs in Micromorphological Thin Sections

# 9.3.1 Distinguishing Parasite Eggs from Botanical and Faunal Remains

The fruiting bodies of coprophilous fungi can be encountered in close association with parasite eggs in micromorphological samples. Specimens from some species, for example coprophilous fungi of the Podospora type, Sordaria type and the root-inhabiting Diporotheca rhizophila (van Geel et al. 2003; van Geel & Aptroot 2006) exhibit morphological similarities with parasite eggs (Figure 9.6). However, spores can usually be differentiated by their smaller size and dark-brown colour (see section 9.2). The unicellular shelled amoeba (Protozoa)

Amphitrella flavum may appear in abundance on the surface of Sphagnum, which was formerly used as 'toilet paper' and thus is often found in context with human faeces. Also having bipolar openings, it resembles Trichuris sp. eggs (Figure 9.6) (B. van Geel 2011, personal communication). Further problems may arise in the analysis of suspected tapeworm eggs because of their morphological similarities with some widespread pollen (e.g., Scabiosa sp.; L. Wick & A. Heitz 2011, personal communication) (Figures 9.7 and 9.5). Another source for misidentification is the decomposition of organic tissues (e.g., roots) where plant cells may fall apart into single units (Figure 9.8). However, accumulations of such components are frequently located next to organic tissues, giving a clue to their origin.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/4b0153f48536d170d6b6b8ba4239bad59f83620d89826581811ae30d1b710fe8.jpg)  
Figure 9.7 Pollen of Scabiosa sp. presenting a radial striated membrane. Depending on the orientation of the cut, such types of pollen may easily be confused with Taenia eggs (pollen collection A.-K. Heitz, University of Basel, Switzerland). PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/fe91a6a50f47b04621c5d7544302954e52168bc55936f74372bd9fd37025392b.jpg)  
Figure 9.8 Cell accumulations probably from a disintegrated root resembling Ascaris sp. eggs found in latrine sediments. PPL. Medieval latrine, Leonhardsgraben, Basel, Switzerland. PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/33e02bcdaf2c97adbbe9ae0facd08d5d5f29643876f49bddf06eee473a541b0c.jpg)  
Figure 9.9 Figures a and b illustrate the effects of different cuts through helminth eggs in micromorphological thin sections. On the left there is a good section of Ascaris sp. presenting all features and permitting identification of the genus. On the right side, an egg of Trichuris or Capillaria was sectioned in such a way that no closer identification is possible. Waterlogged Roman latrine, Kempraten, Switzerland. (a) PPL; (b) XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/d6a62e7b5f0cc68c739e0c896e9212033d85c8b2224cca0b6c63be651f1470a7.jpg)

# 9.3.2 Random Cut of Parasite Eggs in Thin Sections

In soil micromorphology the coincidental cut through the investigated sample implies a random sectioning of the components of a thin section. This hampers the identification of eggs of intestinal parasites, given that this is carried out by evaluating the eggs' size and external structure. In a hairworm egg cut transversally, the typical shape, the bipolar opercula plugs and the original size would be missing (Figure 9.9). The characteristic undulated surface would not be present. If the majority of characteristic attributes are missing, specification of helminth eggs is not possible in thin sections.

# 9.3.3 Alteration of Surfaces

# by Taphonomic Processes

The preservation of helminth eggs depends on the rapid interruption of decay. Compaction of sediment layers, swelling and shrinking, transportation by water (Bouchet et al. 2003) and exposure to fire can change the appearance of parasite ova, damaging the outer membranes and impeding precise identification. The identification of Ascaris eggs requires preservation of their characteristic membranes and is not possible if only the nucleus is preserved. In that case, only the mere presence of parasite eggs can be noted.

# 9.4 Conclusion

Even though parasite eggs are time and again identified in micromorphological studies of archaeological sediments, there are only few systematic searches for such ova (Courty et al. 1989; Macphail et al. 1990; Rentzel 1998; Brombacher et al. 1999; Akeret & Rentzel 2001; Macphail et al. 2008; Macphail & Goldberg 2010; Pichler et al. 2014). Observations are mainly limited to locations containing coprogenous material (e.g., latrines, faeces) even though eggs might also occur beyond such contexts due to water displacement (Pichler et al. 2014).

The occurrence of isolated parasite eggs in sediments confirms the former presence of coprolites, even if these are weathered or have disappeared. Isolated from the faeces, the determination of the parasite species and infected host is nearly impossible in micromorphological thin sections. However, if morphology and morphometrics are available, the identification of the genus generally succeeds. For palaeoparasitology, micromorphology can only produce limited and rather random observations. However, micromorphology allows for the detection of parasite eggs in circumstances where they are unexpected and can thus help to initiate palaeoparasitological investigations by conventional techniques (see Bouchet et al. 2003).

# 10

# Earth Construction Materials

David E. Friesem<sup>1</sup>, Julia Watetz<sup>2,3</sup> and Marylise Onfray<sup>3,4</sup>

<sup>1</sup> McDonald Institute for Archaeological Research, University of Cambridge, United Kingdom  
$^{2}$  UMR 5140 Archéologie des Sociétés Méditerranéennes, CNRS, Université Paul Valery; Ministère de la Culture, INRAP, France  
<sup>3</sup> Département DMOS, Agro-Paris-Tech, Thiverval-Grignon, France  
4 Université Paris 1-Pantheon-Sorbonne, UMR 8215-Trajectoires, France

# 10.1 Introduction

Two groups of techniques are usually distinguished in earth-based construction. (i) Indirect shaping, with the earth materials shaped into a mould such as adobe or mud brick or sundried brick (Aurenche 2003). In the New World, Africa and Eastern Asia, earth construction materials are generally termed adobe. (ii) Direct shaping, including: the cob technique (bauge in French or tauf in Arabic), in which materials are piled by layers, by clumps or by lumps, rammed earth within a temporary frame (pise in French, tapia and tabby in English), daub (also known as wattle and daub) – a term used to address the application of earth material on frameworks or walls (Chazelles 1997), and turf or sod construction (which is dealt with by Huisman & Milek 2017, this book). Goldberg & Macphail (2006) distinguish between building clay (e.g., brickearth) – referring to the use of unworked clay – and worked and transformed earth-based building materials such as mud bricks, daub and rammed earth. The archaeological aim of micromorphological investigation of earth construction materials is to provide sourcing, technological data and the reconstruction of human behaviour embedded in these human-made materials. Key micromorphological features of mud bricks, cob and wattle and daub are described in this chapter.

# 10.2 Mud Bricks

Mud bricks are prepared by mixing soil and / or sediment with vegetal temper. In some cases the bricks can be fired in kilns in order to increase their strength and durability prior to construction (kilns and baked mud bricks were

already in use during the third millennium BC in the urban sites in the Indus Valley – Kenoyer 1998; Possehl 2002). The raw material is usually collected from the settlement's vicinity (Canaan 1932; McIntosh 1974; Goldberg 1979; Rosen 1986; Morgenstein & Redmount 1998; Nodarou et al. 2008). Loamy soils and sediments are best suited for mud brick production. These raw materials vary in particle size and carbonates, as well in the quantity of organic matter added to the raw soil and/or sediments (Rosen 1986; Morgenstein & Redmount 1998). The amount of clay is crucial because it acts as a binder and increases the durability of the brick, yet clay contents above  $50\%$  may cause cracks and shrinkage with drying (Rosen 1986). Materials such as sand, straw, dung, gravel, pottery and carbonates from ash can be added to the raw material in order to improve the mechanical strength of the brick (Torraca et al. 1972; McIntosh 1974; Morgenstein & Redmount 1998). Once the bricks are dry, they can be laid one on top of the other with or without mortar between them. At the final building stage, the mud brick wall can be plastered, usually with mud.

The micromorphology of mud bricks presents high variability in raw materials and production techniques. Yet, some micromorphological studies of mud bricks showed that, as an anthropogenic product, mud bricks possess distinctive micromorphological features, which differentiate them from unworked natural soil and sediments materials (Goldberg 1979; Stoops & Nijs 1986; Matthews et al. 1997; Friesem et al. 2011, 2014a). One of the key micromorphological features in intact and preserved mud bricks is the presence of elongated organic plant/vegetal fragments as well as randomly oriented channels and vugs, which reflect the intentional addition of straw and chaff during brick preparation. Goldberg

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/eec1ff38ab713a2f1ab114a2967cd1ca9fa6c87d7c0848aba4de13b7bc0d5833.jpg)  
Figure 10.1 (a) Thin section of intact preserved mud brick. Note the dense compact structure, low porosity and the moldic voids after fibrous vegetal temper decay marked by the arrow. Bronze Age burnt mud brick. (b) Randomly oriented moldic channels and vugs. Note the broken straw due to rotation pressure during brick preparation. Moldic voids indicating the intentional addition of straw and chaff during brick preparation. Modern context. PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/24b026ab491b2c1f6386e918457264741f7e964141bdad798047d7a892816287.jpg)

(1979) reports that the most distinctive micromorphological features of intact mud bricks are the moldic voids after the fibrous vegetal temper in the mud brick matrix has decayed (Figure 10.1a) (see also Matthews et al. 1997). Most mud bricks will have at their mesoscopic scale a compact structure, decreased porosity (in comparison to the soil or sediments used as their raw materials) and a relatively homogenous distribution of the coarse fraction (Figure 10.1a). In thin section, mud bricks often possess a massive microstructure and a porphyric  $c/f$  related distribution. In addition, due to the mechanical manipulation of the raw material by humans, mud bricks may possess unique micromorphological features resulting from processes such as pugging (mixing/kneading of the raw material with temper and water) (Friesem et al. 2014a). These processes will, in turn, be evident by the presence of pressure-induced deformation (i.e., shearing, rotation and directionality of flow - Figure 10.1b).

These observations are crucial for identifying mud bricks as a worked/ prepared construction material different from unworked natural soils or sediments. These features tend to be better preserved in burnt mud bricks rather than unburnt bricks (see Friesem et al. 2014a), as the latter undergo degradation processes (see below).

# 10.2.1 Degraded Mud Brick Material

Since the early days of archaeology, field archaeologists used the term 'mud brick material' to describe sediments that macroscopically seemed to possess characteristics

similar to those of intact mud bricks (texture, colour and hardness) but that probably resulted from mud brick degradation. Several geoarchaeological studies attribute the formation of mounds to the degradation process of mud-brick structures (Davidson 1973; McIntosh 1974; Rosen 1986; Friesem et al. 2014b). Early stages of degradation may occur while mud bricks are still emplaced in the wall (i.e., in situ degradation) with water and salts acting as major agents of degradation (McIntosh 1974; Fodde 2007). The rate of the process differs according to the amount of precipitation (rain) whereas coarse and fine grains are mobilized by a form of overland flow (Friesem et al. 2011, 2014a). Initial disaggregation and sealing of mud brick surfaces are caused by rainsplash (see Morgan 2005 for similar effect on soil surfaces). This, in turn, initiates a variety of flows, which are classified according to the discharge involved in sediment transportation, the size of translocated clasts and water energy/velocity (Jakob & Hungr 2005). A variety of flows may operate simultaneously resulting in slope depositional processes of the degraded mud brick sediment (for more details on flows acting on degraded mud brick materials see Friesem et al. 2014a). Postabandonment activity, and particularly cultivation, was proved to contribute significantly to the erosion of tell and mound sites (Davidson et al. 2010).

Micromorphologically, the identification of degraded mud brick material relies on evidence for slope deposition and identification of flow processes (Friesem et al.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/2cfb1c644162f2f10cf4f1c0d04e3f20f9babfeea76ac9dd9c6205642c30200d.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/1a00c45e4571c194f5f01f1e27521abb6896f69a3d634f8c02ab80a496345a5d.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/9faeaa967646e74807d4726e5fd2dfeb1ff0e7ea1188d51078d37f3500abea34.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/11fe3cc665f2ce5fbbcf772e2a2f6b517c5b0d1a86e7e2db076965d7524bc65c.jpg)  
Figure 10.2 Degraded mud-brick sediment in a temperate environment. (a) Thin section showing an open structure with vesicles and chambers. (b) Porphyric related distribution with open complex microstructure. PPL. (c) Continuous clay coatings around clasts that are embedded in the sediment matrix. This feature results from debris flow. PPL. (d) Linear arrangements of subbounded clasts, associated with a sorted silty groundmass, which can be attributed to differences in flow regimes and energies. PPL. Image courtesy of P. Karkanas. (e) Same as (d), XPL. Image courtesy of P. Karkanas. All images are from modern contexts.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/04b70ecc2f24c54627d81f29264a111e61519e5d13879b7faab651201f44f4bd.jpg)

2011, 2014a). The overall structure of degraded brick talus/infill sediments is less massive and more open than those of intact mud bricks, but in most cases still possesses a porphyric c/f related distribution with complex microstructure and traces of crude bedding due to flow processes (Figures 10.2a and b). In temperate environments, degraded mud-brick sediment will present indicators of various flows due to greater precipitation in comparison to semiarid and arid environments (Friesem et al. 2011, 2014a). Various flow events can be identified by different micromorphological features. Changes in sorting and weak bedding of clasts oriented with the talus inclination can be used to indicate dry grain flow (see also Mücher et al. 2010 for micromorphological features in dry grain flow). Debris flow will result in the presence of continuous clay coatings around clasts that are embedded in the sediment matrix – also known as 'rolling pedofeatures' (Boschian 1997; Angelucci & Zilhão 2009) (Figure 10.2c). Additional features related to debris flow are the absence of packing voids, which indicates a wet rather than dry grain flow (Postma 1986; Bertran & Texier 1999; Pierson 2005) and porphyric-related distribution (i.e., coarse grains 'floating' within a fine matrix) associated with vugs and vesicles. Due to the low energy of hyperconcentrated flow, such as from

low energy sheet wash, sorting of clasts is minimal. Hyperconcentrated flow can be observed by the presence of a silty groundmass, sometimes interbedded with clay-supported silt due to localized translocation of clay leaving a concentrated silty fraction (Friesem et al. 2014a). Discontinuous sedimentary structures can be attributed to differences in flow regimes and energies (Figure 10.2d and e) (Bertran & Texier 1999; Pierson 2005).

In dry environments, wind-blown material contributes to the infilling of degrading mud structures. Sediments in such sites may be composed of a mixture of wind-blown sediments and degraded mud brick material (Figure 10.3a). In addition, wind abrasion plays an important role along with water in the degradation process of the mud bricks (Torraca et al. 1972; Friesem et al. 2011). In dry areas the coarse fraction that originates from bricks moves gravitationally along the slope (i.e., dry flow), evident by the formation of microscopic laminae composed of coarse sand (Figure 10.3b). With the addition of water in rain events, debris and hyperconcentrated flows also occur in dry environments as indicated by dusty clay – and in some cases limpid clay – coatings and infillings and graded bedding (Figure 10.3c). In dry environments cycles of graded bedding suggest distinct flow events (Figure 10.3d). In temperate environments

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/3221d21c7be9095c4451887e0d195f7ade1b1c7c274ec02dbbfb7b65f93d6c23.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/d81acd1dc3043d91d51553dda0fce9a0f61eda7762c21cfe5ff511ab1e98291b.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/b24be80b80ffb1780315d03df0a8a11b849527b5ffaa10a51576f2760e38e25e.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/13f9f20c85b74350cba34ea9c86d405e195b16068642fd24909c161dd99595e1.jpg)  
Figure 10.3 Degraded mud-brick sediment in an arid environment. (a) Alternating inclined brownish clay-rich thin layers, indicating mud slurry cycles of decayed brick material, in a greyish matrix composed of windblown sediment mixed with mud brick material. Iron Age infill sediment of degraded mud brick structure. (b) Bands of medium sand-sized grains within finer-grained windblown sediments, indicating gravity dry flow of quartz grains from walls following wind abrasion. Modern context. PPL. (c) Porphyric related distribution with open complex microstructure without moldic voids. Note the formation of clay crescent coatings, infillings, graded bedding and differences in sorting within the matrix, indicating debris and hyperconcentrated flows. Modern context. PPL. (d) Graded bedding indicating cycles of rain and/or eolian events. Note that medium and coarse sand grains, deposited due to gravity flow, are 'floating' on silty-clay laminae indicative of mud slurry flows during rain events. In addition, note the disruption of bedding due to bioturbation and the inclination of the thin clay layer indicating slope deposition. Iron Age infill sediment of degraded mud brick structure. PPL.

flow events are almost impossible to discern (possibly due to lower flow energies that relate to a larger amounts of precipitation throughout the year).

Plant roots and the burrowing activity of animals cause serious bioturbation to degraded mud brick sediments and are witnessed by the presence of channels, chambers and vesicles (for micromorphological description of bioturbation effect on degraded mud brick materials see Goodman-Elgar 2008; Davidson et al. 2010; Friesem et al. 2011, 2014a). Frost activity may produce micromorphological pedofeatures such as localized platy structures with deformation features often embedded in a matrix with single-grain sand and silt (for micromorphological description of frost features see Van Vliet-Lanoë et al. 1984; Bertran & Texier 1999; Van Vliet-Lanoë 2010). Postdepositional processes, such as biological activity within the sediments (Goodman-Elgar 2008; Friesem et al. 2011) or human activity as in the case of agriculture (Davidson et al. 2010), may damage the indicative micromorphological features formed by the flow processes described above (Figure 10.3d). Tillage erosion of the mound would

result in lack of lenses of finer washed material and homogeneity of the redeposited soils (Davidson et al. 2010; Friesem et al. 2014a).

# 10.3 Cob

Cob is a construction technique that implies the use of earth materials with a load-bearing role. Moist earth materials are directly shaped in situ (the hand-building technique) using two main different manufacturing processes: piling of hand shaped elements (e.g. lumps) or superposition of earth layers, composed of small clumps  $(4 - 5\mathrm{cm})$ . The cob technique is well identified in archaeological sites in temperate contexts, from the Neolithic to the Medieval period (Jallot 2003, 2009; Chazelles 2007; Gutherz et al. 2010). It is also known as 'earthen walling' or 'clay walling' (Patte & Streiff 2007).

Some micromorphological studies on earth-based construction techniques, including cob, have been carried out in order to provide a better understanding of different steps in the building process in both protohistoric and

historic contexts (Cammas 2003; Goldberg & Macphail 2006; Cammas & Roux 2007; Roux & Cammas 2010). Micromorphological features related to processing and shaping of earthen materials were first described in several Neolithic settlements (Wattez 2003, 2009).

As for mud brick, cob is generally prepared from sediments collected from geological and pedological contexts close to the settlement: marls, alluvial deposits or different horizons of soils such as Cambisols or Luvisols (i.e., cambic horizon and Bt or E horizon in Luvisols), and anthropic sediments from occupation surfaces. The texture is commonly silt loam, with a small to moderate fraction of very fine to fine sands and a low

clay content. The first step in preparation is the mixture of this material with water, as described succinctly by Goldberg & Macphail (2006). This process leads to a more or less homogeneous groundmass depending on the degree of blending and on the sediment-to-water ratio. The microstructure can vary from massive to complex, characterized by the coalescent arrangement of rounded or irregular clumps, which are composed of densely packed microaggregates (Figure 10.4a and b). Porosity is poorly developed, with some vesicles or thin, curved, planar voids (Figure 10.4b). This kind of organization and the presence of infillings of dusty clay in the packing voids (Figure 10.4c), together with dusty clay

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/6edc943d6d34a7386f1fd478e70b70f4c3d2f40c5f8b85d77ba5a813eb418f82.jpg)  
285μm

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/20782ab7dbeb4eb050aecec21f5f200007513ddf04c3d2f98fc3c3c486a3906d.jpg)  
$570\mu \mathrm{m}$

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/0edf005e72e73e8d9bcfea97edf7290d562f163eddf4a5a68525701c1d14f163.jpg)  
$570\mu \mathrm{m}$

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/7aa61cdfc352e08efc1757298b0c96d4c550445d511aada81e9ddfbbe1130dc3.jpg)  
Figure 10.4 Mixing process and tempering. (a) Densely packed microaggregates resulting from a well-mixed calcareous loam with abundance of charcoal indicating the use of occupation floor sediments as raw material. PPL. (b) Clumps with a massive microstructure, consisting of an admixture of fine silt (quartz) and dusty silty clay, from eluvial and clay-enriched horizons of a Luvisol in various proportions. PPL. (c) Vegetal temper: random distribution of numerous elongated and rounded voids, note the dusty clay infillings (arrow) related to partially waterlogged conditions during processing. PPL. (d) Mineral temper: random distribution of abundant coarse sand-sized limestone fragments. PPL.  
$570\mu \mathrm{m}$

and silt coatings around the microaggregates, are features deriving from mixing in wet or partially water-saturated conditions.

During the mixing, some inclusions such as coarse sands or gravels and aggregates from occupation floors / soils (which can be identified by the dotted micromass, small charcoal particles and microartefacts) can be embedded. One of the principal characteristics of cob is the presence of many elongated or rounded voids, related to decomposed vegetal temper such as reeds or straws (Figure 10.4c) (Goldberg & Macphail 2006). Nevertheless, the vegetal temper is not a distinctive characteristic as for mud bricks. In some cases, inclusions of coarse / medium sand or gravels with homogenous distribution in the groundmass can be interpreted as temper (Figure 10.4d). In other cases, especially for small, hand-shaped elements, mineral or vegetal temper is absent.

The shaping process is similar to the technique of clay-coil ceramic or modelling. The mixture can be wet or muddy. At low magnification, the latter is characterized by a composite microstructure, defined by well-accommodating clumps (Figure 10.5a). Imbricated arrangement of clumps is another key that could be helpful for micromorphological identification (Figure 10.5b). These characteristics can be observed in manufactured hand shaped quadrangular or

rounded 'bricks' or lumps, and even in small worked clods that compose walls made by superposed cob layers. Thin parallel planar voids on the edge or at the bottom of these elements is another significant micromorphological feature, indicating use of wet materials during the shaping of the wall (Figure 10.5c).

# 10.4 Wattle and Daub

Unlike mud bricks and cob, earth in wattle and daub construction does not have a load-bearing role, but rather an infilling purpose for the timber framework. The frame is generally composed of a wattle of boughs or reeds interlaced on small poles inserted into the ground (Arcelin & Buchsenshutz 1985; Chazelles 1997). Miromorphological analyses on wattle and daub fragments focus on the reconstruction of the technology adopted. Micromorphological research on this subject in Europe is at a starting point (Cammas 2003; Duvernay 2003; Onfray 2012, 2014; Labille et al. 2014).

The nature of the sediments used for daub is extremely variable. Raw materials come from different sedimentary formations, such as alluvial and marsh deposits and soil material quarried from Cambisols or Luvisols (E and Bt

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/3cd10302a85d2080d1672f57a79441eceb38e7cd1c3569410276b02e1dc7f2da.jpg)  
Figure 10.5 Handworked element or lump, with evidence for shaping and installation. (a) Transverse section (whole slide) of hand-shaped 'brick' showing granular microstructure, with fine curved planar voids. (b) Accommodated clumps with an imbricated organization derived from shaping. PPL. (c) Planar voids related to the compaction process, produced by deposition of one hand-shaped element on another, during construction in wet conditions. PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/348cf68dca36e2301d3c4941a97b9e243c190bfb499751161129d2a4157c1edb.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/b1be5c91537611fe72540634b02517e6615435a7f770ce75bc184bcd4f6dc9fc.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/bc0327a22fa7517544334332d68b2b5632996cff0a638457cd915be621e2bda0.jpg)  
Figure 10.6 (a) Phytoliths and diatoms embedded in silty clay of alluvial origin. PPL. (b) Heterogeneous groundmass indicating weak homogenization: red silty clay aggregate (A) mixed with orange reworked clay aggregate (horizon β) (B). PPL; (c) Vegetal temper: moldic circular or oval voids in silty clay groundmass (alluvial material). Massive microstructure related to homogenization in wet condition. PPL. (d) 'Mineral' temper: burnt angular reworked aggregate, included in the mixed earth material. PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/540a7e80994d08e7decab1748a487f3ef3db4ea9220d2aab798bb63ab86f8bdc.jpg)

horizons) (Figures 10.6a and b, 10.7 and 10.8). The texture ranges from loamy to silty clay, with few fine to medium sands. Unlike mud bricks and cob, raw materials are rarely collected from the immediate substratum/pedosedimentary context of the settlement but are commonly extracted from the surrounding environment, generally less than  $5\mathrm{km}$  away (Onfray 2012, 2014).

The processing of wattle and daub includes the mixing of clay loam and silty clay with water and temper (mineral or vegetal). The massive microstructure associated with a weakly developed porosity indicates strong homogenization under wet conditions (Figure 10.7b). In contrast, some wattle and daub remains show a heterogeneous groundmass, including natural aggregates, indicative of inadequate mixing (Cammas 2003) (Figure 10.6b).

Protohistoric examples in literature and Neolithic case studies show that traditional tempering plants, like straw and reeds, are seldom preserved (Cammas 2003;

Duvernay 2003). In these cases the worked groundmass has moldic circular or oval voids (15 to  $20\%$ ), from 250 to  $500\mu \mathrm{m}$  in diameter, corresponding to former grass stalks (Figure 10.6c). Paleobotanical studies frequently highlight chaff addition, observed at both the macroscopic and microscopic scale (Bonnaire 2008). In some cases, inclusions of burned clumps with uniform size and morphology are observed (Figure 10.6d). The abundance and homogeneous distribution of such inclusions can be interpreted as the outcome of the addition of temper to the grog as in ceramic manufacture (chamotte in French). More commonly, no temper is added during the processing of raw materials.

At low magnification, the massive microstructure and the subhorizontal organization of silts and sands, as well as of subparallel planar voids, indicate the input of a mixture of material in the wet state and applied successively on the wattle (Figures 10.7a and b). Imprints of wattle

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/03faf729515c4d83a7ff5c1b613af8098f566bd9d3bc8fd204ac5b1218cd0d62.jpg)  
Figure 10.7 (a) Thin section scan of a Neolithic wattle and daub fragment: smoothed surface and wattle imprint. (b) Horizontal alignment of silt and parallel planar voids related to the smoothing of the surface. PPL. (c) Wattle imprint characterized by thin subparallel planar voids and microlayered distribution of sands in the groundmass. PPL.

can be seen as concavities at the edge of fragments. The adjacent groundmass shows subparallel planar voids following the orientation of the imprint. On the surface, the subparallel organization of silt and sand reflects the practice of smoothing (Figure 10.7b).

Another technique involves the use of daub to infill between and around the wooden elements of the timber framing (Figure 10.8a). In this case, the daub ground-mass is characterized by a heterogeneous and coalescent arrangement of coarse clumps (Figure 10.8b). Fine subparallel planar voids indicate a high compaction during the application (Figure 10.8c). Fine layers of mixed muddy sediments are applied on the external side (Figure 10.8d).

# 10.5 Conclusion

Raw materials used for earth construction materials depend on local soil and sediment resources available in each locality. Micromorphological properties differ significantly among earth construction materials, mainly

due to the variety of manufacturing techniques: preparation (e.g., water content, temper, mixing), shaping (moulding, modelling and application), and walling. Archaeological earth construction materials are studied through a variety of geoarchaeological methods, mainly micromorphology but also by particle size analysis (e.g., Davidson 1973; McIntosh 1974; Rosen 1986; Homsher 2012; Love 2012); Elemental analysis using XRF (e.g., Emery & Morgenstein 2007; Nodarou et al. 2008; Friesem et al. 2011; Goodman-Elgar et al. 2015), ICP-AES (e.g., Davidson et al. 2010) and Fourier-transform infrared spectroscopy (e.g., Berna et al. 2007; Friesem et al. 2011, 2014a, 2014b; Forget et al. 2015).

Overall, there are some features that allow the identification of earth construction material: (i) the microstructure, which differs from the local soil or sediment, used as raw materials; (ii) evidence of added materials (e.g., remains of the temper materials or indicative void types after the vegetal temper has decayed); and (iii) association of different fabrics of soils and sediments, including anthropogenic sediments, reworked mainly by the manufacturing process. The degradation of earth

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/e4f3fc9ead3494d99d2d2b8007d8d590a30687cf6a2fbe3d91a95b83370bc997.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/fd2fc8d822162abbd33d142d1e4b35ffab91ce208fa49840e89f4ba427f1dbff.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/a5efb60e434cf5c930407cec35976ea0b8d25d3397b83ad284ac58f29d69e508.jpg)  
Figure 10.8 (a) Thin section scan of a Neolithic wattle and daub fragment used for levelling of the wall surface. (b) Wattle wood frame: loosely packed coarse clumps from various earth materials such as yellow silty clay (A) and orange silty clay (B). PPL. (c) Mixed silty clay with ferruginous impregnations inherited from marsh deposits. The thin dusty clay intercalations, with parallel orientation along the edge of the fragment, are related to wattle compression. PPL. (d) Smoothed surface (S): regular microlayers of fine material deriving from successive applications. PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/91d6fa6ec233b5e928d9a155b30b85541dc36cf00b4d162c78211217abaef28c.jpg)

construction material is an important part of archaeological site-formation processes in general and in particular contributing to the formation of archaeological mounds (Davidson 1973; McIntosh 1974; Rosen 1986; Friesem et al. 2014b). The most distinctive micromorphological features for identifying this process are

slope-deposition features and evidence of low energy flows. However, bioturbation and postabandonment activity such as cultivation may disrupt the micromorphological features resulting in a relative homogeneity of the redeposited sediments (Goodman-Elgar 2008; Davidson et al. 2010; Friesem et al. 2011, 2014a, 2014b).

# 11

# Laterite as Construction Material

Georges Stoops

Vakgroep Geologie, Faculteit Wetenschappen, Universiteit Gent, Belgium

The term 'laterite' was introduced by Buchanan (1807) to name a more-or-less clayey material quarried in southern India that hardens rapidly when exposed to air and sun. The term is derived from the Latin word later, which means 'brick'. Later the term was used and misused for a range of reddish soft and hard soil materials in the tropics. The specific soft material that hardens on exposure has been renamed 'plinthite' in soil taxonomy (Soil Survey Staff 1975).

Laterite, in the original meaning, has been widely used as construction material in southeast Asia throughout history. Examples of monumental buildings are the Angkor Wat temple in Cambodia (twelfth

century) where it was used as a core for walls, covered by limestone, the temples of Ayutthaia in Thailand (fourteenth to sixteenth century - Uchida et al. 1999) and large colonial constructions in Malakka, Malaysia. Hard lateritic gravel is still used for road building.

Laterite has a very complex composition and fabric. In general, the main components are residual quartz, often corroded, kaolinite, hematite and goethite. Typical fabric elements are thick microlayered kaolinite-goethite coatings (Figure 11.1), and hypocoatings of microgranular hematite (plinthitic hematite). For more details see Stoops & Marcelino (2010).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/11f40fb7d5194ce5ef6b66182ff4d64495655720acf5563c0f5665a5f3049aba.jpg)  
Figure 11.1 (a) Complex fabric of polygenic laterite (comparable to that of the temple of Angkor Wat) showing old, dark, rounded lateritic gravels surrounded by reddish brown coatings of kaolinite and goethite, and a more recent interstitial groundmass. PPL; (b) other view of same thin section, PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/f16e8feeac5607ca78e520715e1ef4ca136d73f1a3232568be9b5330bf953de6.jpg)

Stoops, G. & Marcelino, V. (2010) Lateritic and bauxitic materials. In Stoops, G., Marcelino, V. & Mees, F. (eds)

Interpretation of Micromorphological Features of Soils and Regoliths. Elsevier, Amsterdam, pp. 329-350.  
Uchida, E., Maeda, N. & Nakagawa, T. (1999) The laterites of the Angkor monuments, Cambodia. The grouping of the monuments on the basis of the laterites. Journal of Mineralogy Petrology and Economic Geology 94, 162-175.

# 12

# Turf as Construction Material

Dirk J. Huisman<sup>1</sup> and Karen B. Milek<sup>2</sup>

<sup>1</sup>Cultural Heritage Agency of the Netherlands, Amersfoort, The Netherlands  
$^{2}$  Department of Archaeology, School of Geosciences, University of Aberdeen, United Kingdom

# 12.1 Introduction

Turves (or sods) are commonly formed by cutting a topsoil vertically into rectangles or strips, after which the densely rooted top of the upper soil horizon is lifted. The root mats of the standing vegetation keep the turves together. Soils with vegetation that form a dense, shallow network of roots in the topsoil are therefore especially suitable for the cutting of turves (Sigurðardóttir 2008). Turves have been used for a variety of goals, including soil-structure amelioration and fertilization (e.g., Sageidet 2009) but in this chapter we focus exclusively on the micromorphology of turves used in construction. Two types of construction are distinguished: domestic structures and larger scale earthworks.

For domestic structures, like houses and stables, turves have been the building material of choice in regions of the world where more common building materials like wood, stone, or clay have been scarce or too great an investment to extract. Domestic structures in which walls and sometimes roofs are made from turves are largely restricted to treeless northern and upland landscapes, for example Iron Age and medieval buildings in Scotland, the Faroe Islands, Iceland, Greenland, the northern coastal areas of Norway, Germany and the Netherlands and early modern buildings in Finnmark, Ireland, Iceland, the Western Isles of Scotland, the Dutch peat areas and the prairie region of North America (Dick 1954; Evans 1969; Voskuil 1979; Walker 2006; Sigurðardóttir 2008). In these constructions, turves could be placed horizontally - usually stacked upside down - but other patterns, including tilted orientations occur also (Figure 12.1). Roof constructions, however, were usually made using turves the right way up so that the grass could continue to live (Goldberg & Macphail 2006; Macphail & Goldberg 2010; Milek 2012).

The use of turves for constructing nondomestic, larger scale earthworks like ramparts, dykes, podia (artificial platforms to enable house construction above flood levels – Nicolaj 2010) and barrows has been much more widespread.

Nonwoody wetland areas such as marshes and peat bogs are especially suitable environments from which to extract turves for construction because they often have dense root mats and because some peats have waterproofing properties (Gestsson 1982; Steinberg 2004; Walker 2006), but turf-based constructions are also known from grasslands and heathlands (e.g., Doornbos 2013; Fontijn et al., 2013), intertidal vegetated areas (e.g., Nicolaj 2010) and moss-vegetated soils (Canti 2009).

In fresh turf constructions, remains of the original vegetation may be preserved (Figure 12.2; Canti 2009). Analyses of pedological and chemical characteristics of turves, surviving botanical remains (both macrofossils and pollen), phytoliths and diatoms may help to determine the source(s) of the turves, and to confirm the use of turves as construction materials on archaeological sites where this interpretation is uncertain (Carter et al. 2006; Bathurst et al. 2010). Moreover, the study of these aspects provides information on soil properties, environment and land use in the source area of the turves at the time of construction (Canti 2009; Macphail et al. 1998, 2013). However, postburial processes may affect the properties of buried turves. Depending on the environment, organic remains – including the root mat – may decay and sometimes diagenetic minerals may form. The following sections first discuss general properties of (fresh) turf-built structures and postdepositional processes that are often associated with them. Subsequently, the value of turf-built structures for environmental reconstruction is discussed.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/04bd5c25071f0ed480e65cc938651ae79db7c225c4ad81ba13c25a762e65da3e.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/bea4b15ad99afbd80f08982df5bc032fe2b90094544a310b42d68a6d953d11aa.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/8ae82303ea8d13c9cf4c57e800e17a1b1f36b71cca2e23255954f466d1a6230d.jpg)  
Figure 12.1 Examples of turf structures. (a) Lengthwise cross-section of a turf-built wall that formed part of a house podium (construction to elevate a house above the flood level). (b) and (c) Turf-lined well with slanted, near vertically placed turves in horizontal (b) and vertical (c) cross-section. Anjum Medieval living mound (terp), Netherlands (see Nicolaj 2010). Images courtesy of J. Nicolaj.

# 12.2 Micromorphology

# 12.2.1 General Properties of Turf-Built Structures and Recognition

Due to destruction or collapse, turf-built domestic structures in archaeological contexts can usually only be studied close to the original ground level (e.g., Huisman 2010; Milek 2012; Milek & Roberts 2013). Little is generally known, therefore, of archaeological turf roofs and upper wall sections except when such materials are still identifiable in collapse layers (Figure 12.3) or by studying reconstructions or preserved (subrecent) turf buildings.

When compared with other types of earthen construction material like mudbrick (Friesem et al. 2017, this book) and laterite (Stoops 2017, this book) fresh turves' main distinguishing features are the presence of a network of roots and/or aboveground vegetation remains that keep the turf together (Figure 12.2). A thin section from an experimentally reconstructed turf house from Scotland built in the 1990s shows such bands of organic tissue (Figure 12.4). Macphail et al. (1998) in their study

of a Roman burial shaft with collapsed turf layers describe  $1.5 - 2\mathrm{cm}$  bands of degraded layered organic matter that may also be remains of aboveground vegetation – although these authors interpret this as herbivore dung. Canti (2009) found moss layers on turves used to construct Sillbury Hill. In other cases, the organic tissue layers may have decayed totally. Sometimes, discrete layers of phytoliths may be left behind after the decay of this material (Figure 12.5). Nørnberg & Courty (1985) describe thin bands that were very rich in phytoliths in the walls of a burned-down turf building and interpreted them as the mineral remnants of completely burnt grasses.

Apart from the remnants of standing vegetation, the properties of the groundmass of the turf structure depend on the properties of the parent soil A-horizon. In many documented cases, turf constructions have high organic matter content and topsoil biological microfabric, including roots, root channels and mesofauna excrements (Goldberg & Macphail 2006). However, there are also numerous sites where the turfs are derived from very weakly developed soils and therefore essentially

consist of vegetated sediment (Canti 2009; Figures 12.1, 12.4). This implies that there are no general soil properties that are typical for turf constructions. Rather, they reflect the variation in topsoils that can be found in those regions where turf constructions have been made.

The fact that turf construction essentially entails (upside down or tilted) stacking of topsoil material means that turf-built structures typically have inverted or tilted and repeating soil horizons and unnaturally sharp boundaries between humic and mineral horizons (Figure 12.6). If plant tissue or phytolith marker layers are absent, these properties (inverted/tilted/repeat topsoil horizons and unexpected sharp boundaries between them) can still be indications of turf-built structures. If they are missing as well, recognizing turves in thin section will be nigh impossible.

# 12.2.2 Taphonomy and Postdepositional Properties

The high organic content of some types of turves also makes them combustible, and burnt-turf constructions are occasionally found on archaeological sites. In thin sections, burnt turves may be expected to have lenses of charred organic remains because organic layers within large turf-built structures often do not have enough exposure to oxygen to fully combust. Still, thin phytolith-rich bands with mineral remnants of completely

combusted grasses are known from walls of burnt-down turf buildings (Nørnberg & Courty 1985). These will consist of charred tissue fragments if the vegetation has not been well humified prior to burning, and charred amorphous organic matter if the vegetation was already well humified prior to burning. There may also be some reddening or blackening of the mineral horizons of burnt turves, depending on the temperature and oxygenation (e.g., Nørnberg & Courty 1985).

Apart from compression due to overburden pressure, two kinds of postdepositional processes are mentioned in relation to turf constructions. Firstly, organic matter decay may occur, especially of the (fresh) buried remnants of the standing vegetation but also of roots and soil organic matter (see above). Secondly, localized reduction of the groundmass may occur in earthworks due to oxygen consumption by decaying organic matter. This process may result in the precipitation of iron oxides on the oxidation-reduction boundaries – sometimes resulting in extreme panning (Breuning-Madsen & Holst 1998; Breuning-Madsen et al. 2000, 2001; Canti 2009) and/or in the impregnation of organic matter with iron oxides and the precipitation of reduced iron species like vivianite (Canti 2009; Macphail et al. 2013). In addition, these reducing conditions are linked to exceptional preservation of organic matter (Canti 2009). Note that these processes are not restricted to turf constructions and can occur in other earthworks.

# 12.2.3 Past Environment Reconstruction

Macphail & Goldberg (2010) describe a range of soil properties - especially decay features of organic material - that may be encountered in turf-built constructions. This may illustrate the value of micromorphological investigations of turf structures in allowing the study of soil properties of the area the turves were derived from at the time of construction. For example, Macphail et al. (2013) in their micromorphological study of the Gokstad burial mound (Norway) found

turves with laminated mull humus type with layers of litter and dung indicators, indicating that the surrounding coastal landscape was a terrestrial sedge grassland managed for grazing. The difference in grain size between silty early medieval Firdgum turves (Figure 12.5) and the clayey ones with thin sand layers used in a modern construction (Figure 12.2) illustrates how the Wadden Sea area intertidal flats have become a more high-energy depositional environment since the construction of sea dikes c. 1200 AD.
