# Plant Remains

Kristin Ismail-Meyer

IPAS - Integrative Prehistory and Archaeological Science, University of Basel, Switzerland

# 13.1 Introduction

Uncarbonized organic remains are found in natural and anthropogenic sediments of waterlogged and dry environments. Poorly preserved organic matter often appears only as a brownish amorphous mass with hardly any visible cell structures. In well-preserved deposits, on the other hand, the anatomy of the plant remains can still be observed. Organic matter in thin sections was first addressed by micromorphological soil scientists who presented overviews of naturally occurring remains in forest litter and its decay products (e.g., Bal 1973; Babel 1975, 1985; Pawluk 1987; Takeda 1988; Tian et al. 1997). Others have focused on the diversity of organic tissues, their general optical characteristics and preservation potential (e.g., Fitzpatrick 1993; Stoops 2003; Stolt & Lindbo 2010). Generally, the identification of organic remains in thin sections can be challenging because of the varying preservation and the random cuts through different sectional planes (radial, transversal, tangential and intermediate stages). Despite these problems, specific characteristics of organic remains allow their identification, thus providing information on the nature of the analysed sediments and further augmenting the taphonomic information inherent in the organics themselves. In this chapter, morphological characteristics of wood, wood bark and twigs, tree and shrub leaves, conifer needles, grasses, seeds, roots and mosses are presented. Plant anatomy reference works aid in the identification of organic remains (e.g., Braune et al. 1983; Gifford & Foster 1989; Schoch et al. 2004; Beck 2010; Upton et al. 2011). For additional information on burnt organic matter see Canti 2017, this book; for decomposing organic matter see Bal 1973; Babel 1975; Pawluk 1987; Tian et al. 1997; Fitzpatrick 1993; Stoops 2003; Macphail & Goldberg 2010; Stolt & Lindbo 2010.

The organic remains presented here originate mainly from central European neolithic lakeside settlements preserved under waterlogged conditions. Remains were identified by comparison with reference thin sections of neolithic archaeobotanical remains, or of modern plants (see Ismail-Meyer et al. 2011).

Morphologically, vascular or higher plants are made up of different types of tissues: the epidermis (single cell layer covering plants), the vascular tissues for conduction (xylem and phloem, see below), and the ground tissue. The latter can be divided into parenchyma (thin walled cells, filler tissue, food storage cells), sclerenchyma (lignified, thick walled cells supporting the plants, as fibres and stone cells), and collenchyma (irregularly thickened cells, such as in leaves and stems) (Babel 1975; Gifford & Foster 1989; Upton et al. 2011). Under terrestrial conditions, many organic tissues, such as parenchyma and collenchyma, decay within weeks or months, whereas lignified and silicified plant tissues, such as sclerenchyma in barks and some seeds (e.g., stone fruits), may be preserved for decades (Gastaldo & Demko 2011). Under waterlogged, anoxic, conditions (e.g., in peat bogs, near lakes or rivers, or where the groundwater table is continuously high and stagnant) even delicate organic materials, such as leaves and mosses, may be well preserved (Kenward & Hall 2000; Ismail-Meyer & Rentzel 2017).

Organic remains normally appear in plane polarized light as yellowish brown in colour. With crossed polarizers, the cellulose of cell walls shows distinct interference colours. Fresh organic remains may be strongly autofluorescent. The latter characteristic is useful in diagnosing the cellulose of fresh plant residues (see Stoops 2017, this book; Altemüller & van Vliet-Lanoé 1990; Fitzpatrick 1993; Stoops 2003; Goldberg & Macphail 2006; Stolt & Lindbo 2010).

# 13.2 Micromorphology

# 13.2.1 Wood, Bark and Twigs from Conifers (Gymnosperms, Pinophyta), Deciduous Trees and Shrubs (Angiosperms)

Generally, the largest part of the wood - the central cylinder or heartwood (xylem) - consists of lignified sclerenchyma with thick-walled cells, containing the vascular bundles for water transport (vessel members;

tracheae and tracheids) and pith rays (Figure 13.1). Especially in the temperate zones, the growth of annual rings is favoured due to changes in growth speed through the year (soft 'early wood' formed in spring and dense 'late wood' in summer and autumn - Schoch et al. 2004; Upton et al. 2011). The xylem has a good chance of being preserved (Stolt & Lindbo 2010). Around the xylem, there is the bark rim, which is divided into two parts. These are the inner bark or sapwood (phloem), which contains sieve plates that transport the photosynthetic

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/aad0b272fc4585443a9faec993892724782eb8200219751d6f58d66ef6e5b7f4.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/b64b41659b5e571aa862b97870b7e583887518f030d9a8978fb7ea371cdece4c.jpg)

products, and the parenchymatic fibres. The thin-walled cells of the phloem decay quickly into yellow, almost structureless masses (Babel 1975; Stolt & Lindbo 2010). Secondly, the outer bark or cork (phellem), which consists of dead sclerenchymatic cells protecting the tree (Figure 13.2). These cells show a characteristic parallel arrangement and are distinctly squashed and deformed. The contained tanning substances (phlobaphenes) make the bark resistant to decay and give it a dark brown to reddish colour (Stolt & Lindbo 2010). Some barks have a thick, yellow epidermis. Bark may contain light

coloured sclerenchmytic stone cells and fibres - Gifford & Foster 1989; Rahfeld 2009).

Conifer wood shows distinct year rings, small vessels (tracheids), uniseriate pith rays, and resin ducts. Wood of deciduous trees and shrubs possesses big vessels (tracheae) arranged in a ring-porous or diffuse-porous fashion, and uni- or multiseriate pith rays. Good sections of wood allow for the identification of the species. Twigs (and small shoots) show one or several year rings protected by a bark layer (Figure 13.3) (see Gale & Cutler 2000; Schoch et al. 2004).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/a32bf63539f9bcf5eac48613f1ebf766c77b5b807a24c47d28da66f7a9850c2a.jpg)  
Figure 13.3 Twigs. (a) Conifer twig (white fir / Abies alba) in transversal section with a small, star-shaped pith (Pi), the xylem (X) showing eight year rings and the small phloem rim (Ph), followed by the outer bark layer (B). Stansstad-Kehrsiten Neolithic lakeside settlement, Switzerland (Ismail-Meyer et al. 2013; Ismail-Meyer and Rentzel 2017). PPL. (b) Same as (a), XPL, showing the interference colours of the cellulose. (c) Deciduous twig in transversal section showing the pith (PI), a small xylem rim (X), the delicate phloem (Ph) and the outer bark (B). Roman waterlogged site. PPL. (d) Same as (c), XPL, showing the interference colours of some cellulose tissues.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/5f2a85639cf3c2c2d8e1762d3e44382cf356624afb7af978b7f4ce70a556acc9.jpg)

Buried under terrestrial (oxic) conditions, wood decays in timeframes of a few years up to a few centuries, depending on its resistance to degradation and the properties of the soil. Buried wood can lose between 50 and  $75\%$  of its tangential volume (recent wood:  $8\%$ ). The xylem and the bark have a good preservation potential, and conifer wood containing resin is generally better preserved than wood of deciduous trees. Wood without its protecting bark is rapidly attacked by fungi and mites (Schweingruber 1976, 1978). For illustrations of degrading wood in thin sections see Blanchette (2000).

Wood in thin sections is yellowish to brown in colour. The cellulose of the cell walls may exhibit interference colours (Schweingruber 1978; Stolt & Lindbo 2010). Under fluorescent light, fresh lignified tissues (xylem) show a blue colour. Upon decomposition, the colour changes to whitish, yellowish and brown shades. Tanned cell walls from bark appear whitish. Parenchymatic tissues (phloem) have a brownish to reddish autofluorescence (Altemüller & van Vliet-Lanoé 1990; Stoops 2003).

# 13.2.2 Leaves of Deciduous Trees, Shrubs and Conifer Needles

Leaves of deciduous trees and shrubs consist mainly of thin-walled parenchymatous cells and show a distinct internal structure. The upper epidermis is followed by an assimilation tissue, separated into the palisade layer and the loose mesophyll containing the leaf veins (vascular bundles), and the lower epidermis with stomata (Figure 13.4) (Upton et al. 2011). Tree and shrub leaves

are observed mainly under waterlogged conditions, but have also been found in cave sites (Akeret & Kuhn 2008; Ismail-Meyer & Rentzel 2014). In thin sections, they appear as elongated, yellowish to dark-brown structures, depending on their state of preservation. The palisade tissue is often the most striking characteristic. The vascular bundles are made up by xylem and phloem, surrounded by a lignified sheath (Braune et al. 1983; Gifford & Foster 1989; Upton et al. 2011). The veins and leafstalks (petioles) may represent the only nondecayed parts. Generally, leaves of trees and shrubs are better preserved than those of herbs. In terrestrial environments, only the leaves' calcium oxalate crystals may survive (see Canti & Brochier 2017a, this book; Franceschi & Nakata 2005). Well-preserved leaves can show interference colours, especially the veins and the cellulose of cell walls (Babel 1975; Fitzpatrick 1993; Tian et al. 1997).

The needles of conifers - morphologically needle-shaped leaves - consist of a parenchymatous tissue (mesophyll) containing one or two central veins (vascular bundles), and are covered by an epidermis with stomata (Figure 13.5). The needles are about 1 to  $3\mathrm{mm}$  wide and distinctly shaped in cross-section: fir needles (Abies) exhibit a 'coat-hanger' shape with two resin ducts and a palisade-like layer. Spruce needles (Picea) are four-sided to diamond-shaped, whereas pine needles (Pinus) have a semicircular shape with two resin ducts on the flat side (Braune et al. 1983; Schoch et al. 1988; Gifford & Foster 1989). Conifer needles resist degradation longer than foliage leaves (Babel 1975; Tian et al. 1997).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/ef6ead64258e5dacf0a46722402c5178830713985c4fe8e4fd6f3fe7d239bf15.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/982df4c44735ec4ad3561c360d2c67f4cbb58e321a8f8dfa19fc3f1788666af0.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/8810cddbbd371714239398a20f4cd5360d4cc4728d0a02c21fe12e7ffa8be3f6.jpg)  
Figure 13.4 Foliage tree and shrub leaves. (a) Cross-section through a fragmented leaf showing the leaf vein (V), palisade (P) and mesophyll tissue (M). Neolithic lakeside settlement Arbon-Bleiche 3, Switzerland (Ismail-Meyer et al. 2013). PPL. (b) Same as (a), XPL, showing the interference colours of the lignified vein (V). (c) Cross-section through several leaves with easily recognizable palisades (P) and a vein (V). Roman waterlogged site. PPL. (d) Birch leaf (Betula): epidermis (E) with a possible stoma (S); mesophyll with a vein (V) and double-layered palisade (P) (identified by M. Kuhn). Wetzikon-Robenhausen Neolithic lakeside settlement, Switzerland (Ismail-Meyer & Rentzel 2017). PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/d849e18fd06c064b1bb7aea44208b4f132d704f80edb82d57b05eb690e15f385.jpg)

# 13.2.3 True Grasses (Poaceae); Leaves and Stems

Grasses are monocotyledons and show a wide range of morphologies (Figure 13.6) (see Ranga Achariyar & Tadulinga Mudaliyar 1921; Gale & Cutler 2000; Akeret & Kuhn 2008). Poaceae leaves are usually elongated in shape and exhibit distinct characteristics in cross-section (e.g., flat, folded or boat-shaped with optional rolled-in edges). Their internal structure differs from deciduous leaves: the veins (vascular bundles) usually consist of two tracheae (xylem) and a larger intercellular space surrounded by parenchymatous and sclerenchymatous cells, the whole resembling the shape of a skull. Usually, there is a larger midrib followed by smaller veins to both sides, all embedded in a spongy mesophyll (parenchymatous cells) and, in contrast to foliage leaves, without palisades. The upper epidermis contains stomata (Braune et al. 1983; Gifford & Foster 1989). The stems of true grasses usually show a round cross-section with an epidermis and a rim of denser collenchyma cells followed by a large-celled ground tissue (parenchyma) with leaf veins ('skulls'), which are usually arranged around the outer rim, and a central pith (Braune et al.

1983, Gifford & Foster 1989). Grasses contain always silica phytoliths, which may be hidden by the organic tissue. Under terrestrial conditions they are often the only evidence for grass remains (see Vrydaghs et al. 2017, this book).

Under the microscope, grass leaves often appear dark brown to black, but are still transparent. They do not display interference colours. The vascular bundles ('skulls') are often the most striking characteristic of grass remains. More thin section pictures of grass remains are to be found in Ismail-Meyer (2014, Figure 7).

# 13.2.4 Roots

Not all subterranean organs are roots; some may be rhizomes (subterranean stems), which show a different structure. Roots are composed of epidermis, cortex with reddish-brown tannins, and conductive vessels in the central cylinder (Figure 13.7). Some parts of roots exhibit root hairs; certain roots are in symbiosis with mycorrhiza; others contain calcium oxalate crystals (Canti & Brochier 2017a, this book; Fitzpatrick 1990; Franceschini & Nakata 2005). Generally, they display various distinct patterns, making an account of their

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/b6270319f6496e3de76e2cc7a980c17ad129d4354071ba8fe51adc4a5a9ed898.jpg)

Figure 13.5 Conifer needles. (a) Cross-section through a white fir needle (Abies alba) showing the typical 'coat-hanger' shape: vein (V), palisade-like tissue (P), spongy mesophyll (M) and two resin ducts (R). Neolithic lakeside settlement Stansstad-Kehrsiten, Switzerland (Ismail-Meyer et al. 2013; Ismail-Meyer & Rentzel 2017). PPL. (b) Same as (a), XPL; only the epidermis (E) and the lignified vein (V) show interference colours. (c) Longitudinal section through a white fir needle (Abies alba), showing the mesophyll (M) and several stomata (S) in the epidermis (E) of the inferior side. Arbon-Bleiche 3 Neolithic lakeside settlement, Switzerland (Ismail-Meyer et al. 2013; Ismail-Meyer & Rentzel 2017). PPL. (d) Same as (c), XPL, showing the interference colours of the epidermis (E) and stomata (S). (e) Cross-section through a spruce needle (Picea abies) with its four-sided shape, the central vein (V), the spongy mesophyll (M), and the epidermis (E) Modern peat. PPL. (f) Same as (e), XPL, with the cellulose of the vein and the epidermis showing interference colours. (g) Longitudinal section through a spruce needle (Picea abies) showing the spongy mesophyll (M) and the epidermis (E), possibly with stomata. Modern peat. PPL. (h) Cross-section through a pine needle (Pinus) with its semicircled shape, double vein (V), epidermis (E), and mesophyll (M). Modern peat. PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/ac25ddac286e4c9c57df0d9dfc1be5d32cf29ed57ecb1b047d0cc079d7a93666.jpg)

Figure 13.6 True grasses. (a) Cross-section through a well-preserved grass leaf (L), possibly Phragmites (Gale & Cutler 2000) with three leaf veins (V, 'skulls'). Note the numerous grass veins in the surrounding sediment. Neolithic lakeside settlement Cham-Eslen, Switzerland (Ismail-Meyer et al. 2013; Ismail-Meyer & Rentzel 2017). PPL. (b) Same as (a), XPL, only one vein (V) shows low interference colours. (c) Cross-section through a grass stem (wheat / Triticum dicoccum - Gale & Cutler 2000). Stansstad-Kehrsiten Neolithic lakeside settlement, Switzerland (Ismail-Meyer et al. 2013; Ismail-Meyer & Rentzel 2017). PPL. (d) Detail of a grass stem (wheat / Triticum dicoccum) showing the epidermis (E), the veins (V), the parenchymatic tissue (P) and the central pith (Pi). Wetzikon-Robenhausen Neolithic lakeside settlement, Switzerland (Ismail-Meyer & Rentzel 2017). PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/f8a70e3938398ca2e18685adaa32ef88b229d12c63352a17e5297a536dd0a6b0.jpg)

general characteristics difficult (on root morphology see Schweingruber 1978; Fitzpatrick 1990; Upton et al. 2011).

The central cylinder of roots is often completely decomposed whereas the cortex may be well preserved because of the tannins (Babel 1975). Frequently, roots are recognized by their 'behaviour' in the sediments, i.e. by their vertical or horizontal alignment rather than by their structure. After decay, the empty ducts may be filled up with different types of sediments (Babel, 1975; Fitzpatrick, 1990; Blazejewski et al., 2005; Stolt & Lindbo, 2010). The identification of root species is difficult (see Hather 1993).

In soil thin sections, roots appear yellowish to brown and may show interference colours. In fluorescent

light, fresh roots show a bluish colour, which gets whitish to yellowish with ageing (Altemüller & van Vliet-Lanoé 1990).

# 13.2.5 Seeds and Fruits

Usually one fruit (pericarp) encloses several seeds. Seeds are often small (<1 mm) and of distinct shape. Fruits and seeds show a complex anatomy, possessing several layers and with different preservation potential. Firstly, the seeds' cell contents and food stores (starch, proteins) decay. Silicified plant tissues, such as those from stone fruits, hazelnut shells (stone cells/sclereids) or charred remains (Kenward & Hall 2000; see also Canti 2017, this book) have the best preservation potential. Seeds and

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/c94805705173abc14e87bd05d223dcfb2a1973703eeea2ec85d797039e34c147.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/c22010b0327edf9b069d7e9b04e379725f6bb8b76ec30ec6058e0736c92e56b4.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/75dc47b52889c4eea4fa203c1260336129151d0145b4ac89e6ce8afdc4f3b423.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/b8aba45f8fa62cacb52066f75a526eb111e832494dc329ceaa0a11b53faf1120.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/0199bc5f0e56e3862b7b8417686cce50f057f51355129effc2623c6b2456e5c5.jpg)  
Figure 13.7 Roots. (a) Root from a peat sediment in cross (Cr) and longitudinal section (L). The rim of the root is darker because of tannins. Modern peat. PPL. (b) Same as (a), XPL, showing the interference colours of the cellulose. (c) Root of a reed (Phragmites sp.), penetrating the archaeological layers of the Cham-Eslen Neolithic lakeside settlement, Switzerland (Ismail-Meyer et al. 2013; Ismail-Meyer & Rentzel 2017). PPL. (d) Same as (c), XPL. The root shows interference colours. (e) Longitudinal section of a root, which brought clay material (C) into a carbonatic sediment. In the lower right corner a burnt bone, possibly of fish, is present. Same site as (c), PPL. (f) Same as (e), XPL. The clay spots (C) are clearly visible.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/4e86c89cf86d7ec1ef6f27675d07e034b72f3d3afab0a96d6185d343708026c5.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/95124d402d4bfb10fd284a505cf8fb2869f2ab2693943caa996d15ab05f2be1d.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/cdde51c0cbc0aaabe51df95a38993147357d31a3b9b78eab3b0b83f0da209741.jpg)  
Figure 13.8 Blackberry (Rubus fructicosus) 'seeds' (botanically termed 'nutlets'). (a) Experimental thin section with several blackberry nutlets in different sectional planes. The characteristic highly ridged endocarp made of stone cells is easily recognized. Thin section courtesy of M. G. Canti. PPL. (b) Same as (a), XPL, the stone cells of the endocarp show interference colours. (c) Detail of two blackberry 'seeds' showing the fibrous structure of the endocarp. Wetzikon-Robenhausen Neolithic lakeside settlement, Switzerland (Ismail-Meyer & Rentzel 2017). PPL. (d) Same as (c), XPL. Note the clearly visible fibres.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/3342decbeb9d8d510e04872e4cdf1841ebbb754a6b40e39e370b0dfbc75b78eb.jpg)

fruits in soil thin sections may exhibit distinct colours from yellow to red to brown. Interference colours of certain tissues may be distinct, especially of stone cells. It may be difficult to recognize the same seeds in different sectional planes; it is therefore important to compare tissue layers, cell structures and colours. Seeds rich in starch burst when heated to 'popcorn', as for example cereal, maize, rice or millet grains (see Canti 2017, this book). The fruits and seeds regularly observed in archaeological sites are blackberry/raspberry (ovate to half-circular, waved shape of  $2 - 3\mathrm{mm}$  length, Figure 13.8), hazelnut shells (spherical to oval in shape,  $15 - 25\mathrm{mm}$  long,  $10 - 15\mathrm{mm}$  in diameter, Figure 13.9), strawberry (may be confused with cinquefoil / Potentilla; botanical identification needed), poppy, flax and burnt cereal

grains (thin sections to be found in Ismail-Meyer 2014, Figures 7 and 8).

Cereal grains (caryopses) have an elongated shape (ca.  $3 - 13\mathrm{mm}$  long) with a brush on the narrower end and a ventral crease (Figure 13.10). Fresh grains contain starch in the form of small rounded grains (size ca.  $2 - 100\mu \mathrm{m}$ ) showing greyish interference colours with an extinction cross (similar to spherulites – see Gassner & Bothe 1973; Canti & Brochier 2017b, this book). The bran (pericarp and testa) displays a distinct structure with several layers, often still recognizable even if the grain is charred. Around the grain the husks may still be present (Gassner & Bothe 1973). The seed coats (testa) are often the only evidence of uncharred cereal grains under waterlogged conditions (Figure 13.10).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/3803aeadbad04e4f583bd427db23be01024e3689f1f31e4567445afa08345294.jpg)  
Figure 13.9 Hazelnut shells (Corylus avellana). (a) Experimental section through the pericarp made of stone cells with the resin ducts (R). At the surface of the pericarp there are short, thick plant hairs (H). The internal part of the pericarp consists of brown, folded cells (C). PPL. (b) Same as (a), XPL, showing interference colours of the stone cells. (c) The hazelnut is still surrounded by the leafy 'husk' (Hu, involucre). Arbon-Bleiche 3 Neolithic lakeside settlement, Switzerland (Ismail-Meyer et al. 2013; Ismail-Meyer & Rentzel 2017). PPL. (d) Same as (c), XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/8a25ff4605bb5e1a6c33e3145b5d4ae79cea288a219c1cc9bf08cd66c5f804ad.jpg)

# 13.2.6 Mosses (Bryophyta: Bryopsida and Sphagnopsida)

Mosses are soft, nonvascular, perennial green plants, which usually grow in wet environments, such as forests and bogs. They possess vegetative bodies with stalks, (nonvascular) winglike leaves and rhizoids, but no lignified cells or vascular bundles. The stalks show a thickened epidermis (sometimes with a rim of thicker, sclerenchymalike cells) and a parenchyma with a central duct. Moss leaves consist of one to three cell layers, usually built up of just one cell type (no epidermis, palisades or veins - Figure 13.11) (Braune et al. 1983; Goffinet et al. 2009; Upton et al. 2011). In thin section it is easy to distinguish between leaf mosses (Bryopsida, Figure 13.11a-c) and turf mosses (Sphagnum; Sphagnopsida, Figure 13.11d-f). Leaf mosses exhibit a yellowish to brown colour in plain polarized light. The rim of the stalks appears darker than the central part. Turf mosses are almost transparent, with just the rim of the stalks having a brownish colour. In horizontal section, moss leaves show specific cell patterns, which may enable the identification of the moss species (see Mauquoy & Van Geel 2007; De Vleeschouwer et al. 2008; Müller & Frahm 2013). Mosses, especially their leaves, decay quickly. The outer part of the stems is better preserved than the central part (Babel 1975). More thin section pictures can be found in Ismail-Meyer et al. (2011, Figure 1) and Ismail-Meyer (2014, Figure 7).

# 13.3 Future Prospects

Little attention has been paid to the recognition of organic remains in micromorphological thin sections, particularly in anthropogenic sediments (exceeds are for example Ismail-Meyer & Rentzel 2004; Todisco & Bhiry 2008; Ismail-Meyer et al. 2013; Ismail-Meyer 2014). The use of their potential other than as a source of taphonomic information allows for further interpretation, in natural environments and in anthropogenic deposits. In the first case, organic remains may be interpreted as remains of plants growing in situ, for example mosses in a peat bog, grasses in a meadow, or deciduous and conifer leaves and twigs as litter in a forest. If organic remains form part of an anthropogenic layer, they allow a wide range of interpretations. These include bark and wood chips as remains of building activities or fuel, or twigs, leaves and straw in combination with coprolites as indicators of animal husbandry. Highly fragmented leaves and stalks can be regarded as evidence for disintegrated herbivore coprolites (see Bronnimann et al. 2017, this book). In the study of construction materials and techniques, mosses, bark and twigs can be found in insulation, bark and twigs beneath compact loam floors as sub constructions, straw in loams as tempering (see Macphail & Goldberg 2010). Blocks of dried peat were used as construction materials for turf houses (see Milek 2012; Huisman & Milek 2017, this book). Lastly, seeds and

fruit remains are observed in the context of agriculture, food gathering and food-preparation activities (Ismail-Meyer & Rentzel 2004; Ismail-Meyer et al. 2013; Ismail-Meyer 2014). The identification of roots also plays an important role in the reconstruction of soil processes.

# 14

# Chaff

Cristiano Nicosia<sup>1</sup> and Matthew G. Canti<sup>2</sup>

<sup>1</sup> Centre de Recherches en Archéologie et Patrimoine, Université Libre de Bruxelles, Belgium  
$^{2}$  Historic England, Eastney, United Kingdom

# 14.1 Introduction

Crop processing generates a series of byproducts: straw, weed seeds, cereal grains and chaff (see Hillman 1981; Bower 1992; Harvey & Fuller 2005). In archaeobotany, the term 'chaff' refers to all the components of the cereal ear other than grain (rachis segments, glume bases, lemma and palea, etc. - definition by van der Veen 1999). In this study, however, we examine a simple mixture of husk (i.e., the thin, scaly bracts enclosing each seed - definition by van der Veen 1999), awns and grains.

On archaeological sites, chaff can be found in a variety of contexts, such as: (i) in ash, when chaff is used directly as fuel or when chaff-rich herbivore dung is burnt (see van der Veen 1999; Campbell & Robinson 2010); (ii) in cereal processing and/or storage areas (Harvey & Fuller 2005); (iii) in the temper of mudbricks or pottery (van der Veen 1999; Forget et al. 2015; Friesem et al. 2017, this book); (iv) in fodder, dung layers or within single coprolites (see van der Veen 1999; Valamoti & Charles 2005; Albert et al. 2008).

Chaff is mostly preserved in the archaeological record when burnt, either incompletely (charred) or completely (ashed, with only phytoliths surviving – see Boardman & Jones 1990).

Micromorphological descriptions of chaff are absent from the literature. An experiment was therefore set up in order to establish the morphology of unburnt, charred and ashed chaff in petrographic thin sections. Spikelets still in their protective husk from einkorn (Triticum monococcum) and emmer wheat (Triticum dicoccum) produced at Butser Ancient Farm (Hampshire, UK) were hand rubbed on a sieve to produce a mixture of husk and awns with some grains and straw fragments. Further liberation of grains was done manually. Such an assemblage is similar to that obtained after the pounding and winnowing stages of crop processing documented in

archaeological and ethnographic contexts (see Harvey & Fuller 2005). The resulting material was placed in a wedge-shaped ceramic pot made in such a way as to ensure a gradation of reduction conditions. When propped up in the furnace, the shape of the pot was such that a build up of  $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$  would develop in the deep end, grading to fully oxidized conditions in the shallower end. After a slow warm up of over  $2\mathrm{h}$ , combustion was carried out at  $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  for  $30\mathrm{min}$ . The whole pot and its contents were then impregnated with resin, cut and mounted as a standard thin section. A sample of unburnt chaff was mixed with quartz sand (in order to allow grinding to  $30\mu \mathrm{m}$ ), impregnated and mounted as a thin section.

# 14.2 Micromorphology

The most characteristic elements of chaff in thin section are those deriving from the cutting of husks along different, randomly oriented, planes. When cut along a roughly tangential/longitudinal or transverse section, the morphology of husk is easily recognized. Aws and straw are harder to identify as their morphology is such that they are hardly ever cut along a longitudinal plane. An example of husk cut along a tangential/longitudinal section is shown in Figure 14.1.

Charring (from burning in reducing conditions in the deep end of the pot) causes the organic component of chaff to become very dark brown/blackish in PPL (Figure 14.2). In completely ashed (fully oxidized) chaff, we only observed articulated silica phytoliths with different morphologies (Figure 14.3 - see Vrydaghs et al. 2017, this book).

Charred grains have a clearly identifiable, distinct morphology (see also Ismail-Meyer 2017, this book). They disappeared in the completely ashed part of the experimental sample. This is probably due to the scarcity of siliceous component in these species' grains.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/6f919c6fdfc305f6805f62f3ffb02e8f07e998787a9d6b68f0c88abcb99d30d5.jpg)  
Figure 14.1 'Fresh'(unburnt) husk fragment cut along a roughly tangential plane. Note first-order interference colours in PPL (see Fitzpatrick 1984). (a) PPL. (b) XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/7ddf7347d9fecc2fd768ffc1090d2f4519746bf9ce474bc62c9972fbd6f748af.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/358af68c6fbcd7b39c06dc4535a993fe7ad6abc5d5bbcef76ea9f39ee93b6637.jpg)  
Figure 14.2 (a) Charred husk cut predominantly along a transverse plane, giving rise to a recognizable 'spiral-like' morphology. PPL. (b) Charred husk cut predominantly along a longitudinal / tangential plane, resulting in elongated fragments. Some fragments cut along a transverse plan are visible in the lower right portion of the image. PPL. Note blackening due to charring (incomplete combustion) in both images.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/0f72d51ee07bc6976024e1b2ab967bfc0b29a85c0eede486ee97f41474b5d558.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/cad8734155e1c1e22e9f7c86d879c49c7a1c8845443d07f21bbe2cdbace0f4fb.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/b0b9ce88823b1b3e00ff274fd736bfb60a076510edebd0fe1956cb200a7a6d71.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/7728a46237af8f632adf6f040c0c719e85f13c9153171dd704733634aec5fca1.jpg)

# 14.3 Conclusion

When cut along roughly tangential/longitudinal or transverse planes, husk fragments have a distinct morphology (see Figures 14.1, 14.2 and 14.3). Charring results in browning or blackening of chaff (Figures 14.2 and 14.3b), while

complete ashing of chaff leaves only the siliceous skeleton behind (Figure 14.3). Both circumstances can be encountered in archaeological ash deposits (Figure 14.4). Further studies are necessary to determine if the morphologies outlined in this chapter are exclusive to chaff or could be found in macroremains derived from other grasses.

15

# Charred Plant Remains

Matthew G. Canti

Historic England, Eastney, United Kingdom

Charred plant material (including charcoal) can come from all plant parts including roots, branches, leaves and trunk wood. When these materials combust, they first lose water and shrink. They then start to give off structural hydrogen and oxygen, which concentrate the carbon, leading to blackening. Charred plant material forms when the air supply becomes temporarily or permanently limited at this stage, such that the oxidation process is curtailed and heat production stops. If this condition is maintained and the fire is not reignited, the plant material is left blackened and shrunken, but often still recognizable to some taxonomic level. Although the restricted view in thin sections makes identification more difficult than a hand specimen, use has been made of charcoal in some micromorphological studies (Goldberg et al. 1994). Normally charcoal in thin section appears as opaque fragments in plane and crossed polarized light, exhibiting a slight metallic lustre in oblique incident light (OIL), with a specific porosity pattern (see below). In archaeological thin sections, charcoal is often encountered as finely (e.g. silt or very fine and fine sand-sized) comminuted particles (see Arroyo-Kalin 2017, this book). Such particles can hardly be differentiated in thin section from 'organic fine substances' (see Babel 1975) or 'organic punctuations' (Stoops 2003) occurring naturally in soils. When larger pieces of wood charcoal are present as a transverse cut, a simple distinction between ring-porous hardwood, diffuse porous hardwood and softwood should be possible (Schweingruber 1982).

Ring-Porous Hardwoods Ring-porous species are distinguished by having large pores (vessels) concentrated in the wood grown during the early part of the year

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/61481aa694a6a15f9176b37bd6ca8c0d68868cf09f803f13546821be3aa5643d.jpg)  
Figure 15.1 Charcoal of oak (Quercus robur). PPL.

(spring wood). Well-known examples are Quercus (Figure 15.1), Fraxinus and Ullmus.

Diffuse-Porous Hardwoods Diffuse porous species have their pores scattered evenly throughout the early and later wood growth (spring and summer wood). This group contains the majority of the porous hardwoods (Figure 15.2).

Softwoods Softwoods have no vessels but consist chiefly of rectilinear cells known as tracheids (Figure 15.3). The species are mainly conifers (gymnosperms) such as Pinus, Abies, Picea, Juniperus and Taxus.

In practice, there are intergrades between the ring-porous and diffuse-porous groupings (Butterfield & Meylan 1980) that are sometimes classified as semidiffuse

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/22ac7f8d69638bdb427b80280375c815da7e0ee40f417b8b0ddf5bc7d929d098.jpg)  
Figure 15.2 Charcoal of field maple (Acer campestre). PPL.

porous and semiring porous. This type of problem makes identification very much the province of a botanist. Furthermore, there may be issues concerning whether blackened plant remains are burnt or humified (see

16

Coal

Matthew G. Canti

Historic England, Eastney, United Kingdom

# 16.1 Introduction

Coal is compressed and chemically altered vegetal material from ancient forests and swamps from the geological past. It sometimes contains visible plant remains and small amounts of sedimentary material, but is frequently altered beyond all recognition. The definition of coal is not easily circumscribed. As alteration becomes less pronounced, coals grade into peats and as mineral content increases they resemble organic-rich shales (see also Liguois 2017, this book).

# 16.2 Types of Coal (after Read 1971)

# 16.2.1 Brown Coal or Lignite

Brown coal is a compact and lustrous variety that still shows traces of vegetable origin.

# 16.2.2 Cannel Coal

Cannel coal is dense and nonlustrous, with a conchoidal fracture. It is mainly composed of spores and pollen and is a sapropelic coal, the other main member of this group being 'boghead' coal, which is largely composed of algae. The two cannot be distinguished in a macrosample (Thomas 1992). Cannel coal was used in the past for beads in the same way as jet (see, for example, Davis et al. 1995).

# 16.2.3 Bituminous Coal

As the name suggests, a high-bitumen variety widely used in domestic fires. It is commonly classified into the subgroups fusain (powdery and charcoal like), durain

(hard and nonlustrous), vitrain (bright with glassy streaks) and clarain (lustrous). These occur together in bands in the coal seam.

# 16.2.4 Anthracite

Anthracite is hard and high carbon, with a lustrous surface and a conchoidal fracture. It burns as a smokeless fuel, and was used in the past as a black pigment (Edmonds et al. 1979). It occurs mainly in areas where the coal seams have been subjected to extreme pressure.

# 16.3 Optical and Morphological Properties

# 16.3.1 Brown Coal or Lignite

A thin section of brown coal is deep reddish orange to opaque in PPL, with plant-growth rings and transverse rays still traced out in dark and light orange patterns (Figure 16.1). It is similar but darker under XPL, with very weak interference colours and hardly any extinctions.

# 16.3.2 Cannel Coal

A thin section of cannel coal (Figure 16.2) shows very dark, often opaque, areas, occasional quartz grains and light-orange pores with complex extinction patterns in XPL.

# 16.3.3 Bituminous Coal

Bituminous coal is mostly opaque in thin section but punctuated by laminae of deep orange organic material in PPL (Figure 16.3), showing extinctions occurring in

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/bb9ba19982d63a84ebf40eb03b6af27ff0b75d842e1847fa850b03e476c67edd.jpg)  
Figure 16.1 Tertiary coal (lignite) from the Isle of Wight, United Kingdom. PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/3116f729a921a7ba368583e6481140044ea54a3bf7c19634d2fcb8f00e423626.jpg)  
Figure 16.2 Cannel coal from the British Coal Board. PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/738e4aa25c84756fedce1d92fa367f7853de29a4aaefc1ef895087d98c06ff71.jpg)  
Figure 16.3 Bituminous coal from Silverdale, Staffordshire, United Kingdom, showing a collapsed megaspore on the right-hand side. PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/cde9bf383b8cfc439996131ebdf9ea9f0a4f8ea89374d84c141de723eb4b121a.jpg)  
Figure 16.4 Commercial anthracite. PPL.

complex waves under XPL. Some laminae completely surround a dark core, like crushed rubber bands. These are collapsed megaspores (Pettijohn 1975; Retallack 1991).

# 16.3.4 Anthracite

Thin sections of anthracite are largely opaque with parallel layers and lenses of thinner material showing deep orange colouration in PPL (Figure 16.4) and masking very weak interference colours under XPL. These areas often have undulose extinction.

# 16.4 Alteration of Coal by Burning

Coal ash is composed chiefly of silica and alumina with smaller quantities of iron and calcium (Barroso et al. 2006). The silica occurs mostly in the form of plant-derived opal with variable amounts of quartz and rock fragments as well. The other major elements are not especially prominent in the thin section, except for pinkish iron staining, which occurs in patches (Figure 16.5). The remainder of the ash is composed of incompletely burnt carbonaceous matter, sometimes showing the remains of plant structure, but also forming characteristic vesicular lumps, which are a distinct stage of the coal-burning process (Figure 16.6; see also Driese et al. 2010).

# 16.5 Archaeological Examples

Coal or coal remains have been found in forts on Hadrian's wall, other Roman military sites, the Welsh forts, Chester, York, Bath and so forth (Webster 1955), as

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/4eb15e5399f16ea7d61e1766209c9e054f740e0f9dac7cc152c0cdee3cb64b1b.jpg)  
Figure 16.5 Coal ash from a domestic fire, Staffordshire, United Kingdom. (a) PPL; (b) XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/099a3fe41e1956e505149f474f042ade310d792237b17f3a5438abd22ee21857.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/5bdeebf4-1532-4cc3-b477-5f45cb6229ea/639ed6e0fe70626029a16e9c52d218ee1ff430eaeab08a5b80b8a0bc7cc92c33.jpg)  
Figure 16.6 Half-burnt coal from a domestic fire, Staffordshire, United Kingdom. Left-hand side is unburnt; right-hand side shows vesicular structure that develops during burning. PPL.

well as at numerous domestic sites throughout the British isles (Dearne & Branigan 1995). Descriptions from thin sections have not been found.

# 16.6 Conclusions

The identification of coal and coal ash in thin section is relatively simple but has hardly ever occurred in archaeological micromorphology. This is probably due partly to the modern bias in coal's use as a fuel but may also simply be due to the lack of optical descriptions in published atlases and papers.

# 17

# Plant Ash

Matthew G. Canti<sup>1</sup> and Jacques Élie Brochier<sup>2</sup>

1 Historic England, Eastney, United Kingdom  
$^{2}$  Aix-Marseille Université (AMU), UMR 7269 LAMPEA, Aix-en-Provence, France

# 17.1 Introduction

Ash is formed when oxidation of plant material in fires has progressed beyond charring, to the point where the carbon, hydrogen and oxygen components are largely vaporized. The previously small quantities of mineral constituents in the plant then become the major component left behind as ash.

# 17.1.1 Ash Composition

The main mineral constituents of plants are silica (siliceous phytoliths) and calcium oxalate (oxalate phytoliths), which exist in different forms and quantities according to the plant families.

Silica is widespread in plants but commonest in the monocotyledons, especially grasses and horsetails, where it can be a large percentage of the ash composition. It takes the form of intercellular and intracellular bodies, either discrete phytoliths (Figure 17.1), multicellular cuticle structures (Figure 17.2) or silica skeletons cementing the whole vascular system (Figure 17.3).

Calcium oxalate (as the monoclinic monohydrate whewellite,  $\mathrm{CaC_2O_4.H_2O}$ ; or the tetragonal dihydrate weddellite,  $\mathrm{CaC_2O_4.2H_2O}$ ) is ubiquitous in most herbs, trees and shrubs but very rare in grasses (Metcalfe 1960; Prychid & Rudall 1999). It occurs in a variety of shapes (see for examples Scurfield et al. 1973; Franceschi & Horner 1980); the commonest types are the prismatic crystals, which are single or twinned individual crystals, typically  $10 - 20\mu \mathrm{m}$  (Figure 17.4) and the druse, which is a  $15 - 50\mu \mathrm{m}$  rosette of radially aligned crystals (Figure 17.5). They are generally not preserved in archaeological sediments, but have occasionally been found protected in degraded plant tissues (Figure 17.6).

# 17.1.2 Ash Formation

On heating, calcium oxalate transforms directly into calcium carbonate at around  $400 - 500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  by loss of CO (Pobeguin 1943; Dollimore 1987; Frost & Weier 2003, 2004). If fires get hotter,  $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$  is driven off leaving CaO, which, on cooling becomes  $\mathrm{Ca(OH)}_{2}$  then recarbonates with atmospheric  $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$  to form  $\mathrm{CaCO}_{3}$ . Experiments with plant calcium oxalates show that the transformation into lime occurs around  $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , which is lower than the conversion temperature measured on synthetic calcium oxalate (Pobeguin 1943; Brochier 1983). Recent experimentation has, however, measured a higher transformation temperature (ca.  $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ) from some plant oxalates (Shahack-Gross & Ayalon 2013), suggesting that influences from other variables are yet to be clarified for this system. The calcium carbonate in ash can be derived through either of these transformation (direct or by recarbonation) routes (Folk 1973; Shahack-Gross & Ayalon 2013).

Although some changes occur during heating (notably the loss of OH groups), silica is mostly unaffected by the temperatures in normal domestic fires, as long as it does not enter into a melt with alkaline salts (see section 17.2). Variable quantities of mainly potassium and sodium salts are present in fresh ash, and these can play a part in subsequent taphonomic changes.

# 17.2 Micromorphology

The transformation of calcium oxalate into calcium carbonate results in the molecular rearrangement of the relatively large oxalate crystals into microcrystalline aggregates of  $\mathrm{CaCO}_3$ , which retain the varied gross

morphologies of the oxalates. Thus, both prismatic and druse pseudomorphs (Figures 17.7, 17.8 and 17.9) may be found intact in ash (Brochier 1983; Brochier et al. 1992; Cailleau et al. 2005).

The pseudomorphs are made up of very small calcite crystals, a few of which can be seen under the highest magnification, but the remainder are submicron in size. The smaller the crystals are, the higher is the proportion of the microscopic view being taken up by the dark crystal outlines. The pseudomorphs are thus darkened almost to opacity in plane polarized light due to compound relief produced by multiple overlying crystals (Figure 17.9a). Under crossed polars (Figure 17.9b), the compound birefringence of the stacked submicron

The submicron crystals are randomly oriented; thus the pseudomorphs never go into extinction when the stage is rotated, although very elongated prisms may occasionally show partial extinction. Under reflected light, they appear as bright white crystalline forms owing to

light diffusion (see white areas in Figure 17.6c). Some oxalate pseudomorphs, frequently observed in archaeological deposits, have slightly larger crystals (microsparite - Figure 17.8). Their shapes are less sharp, and they are sometimes difficult to recognize. They have never been produced experimentally and the formation process is still unclear, although some type of recrystallization seems most likely (Brochier & Thinon 2003).

Although there are numerous forms of calcitic pseudomorphs after oxalate (as numerous as the crystal habits of plant whewellite and weddellite), very few of them can be attributed to a specific taxon. It is, however, possible to recognize, on morphometric grounds (Brochier 1991, 1995), the genus Pinus (very elongated prisms) and the two species (cordata and platyphyllos) of the genus Tilia (elongated and short prisms associated with druses). See Figures 17.10, 17.11 and Table 17.1.

negative relief of opaline silica (R.I. typically 1.41-1.46) is frequently noticeable on the surfaces and sculpturing of phytoliths in plane polarized light. Under crossed polars, silica phytoliths are isotropic (Figure 17.12).

Although the gross morphology of opaline silica is largely unchanged in ash, heating phytoliths does alter the refractive index by removing the water and hydroxyl groups (Elbaum et al. 2003). The effect must technically be visible in thin sections but the range of RI differences is quite small (ca. 0.05) and measuring it requires deliberate sample preparation in refractive oil. Phytoliths can be darkened by burning but some are naturally dark without any heating (Parr 2006). These colour differences are thought to be due to occluded carbon, which can also provide valuable radiocarbon dates (Wilding

1967; Mulholland & Prior 1992), but see Santos et al. (2012) for problems with this approach.

Silica can undergo significant morphological alteration by fusion. This happens when the ash contains a high enough proportion of fluxing agents such as sodium and potassium, which lower the overall melting point of the mixture. The resulting material ranges from a lightly fused cast of the original plant silica skeleton through to lumps of glassy clinker-like material, usually vesicular (Figure 17.13) due to gas bubbles developing in the liquid melt (Vélain 1878; Folk & Hoops 1982; Thy et al. 1995; Guélat et al. 1998; Brochier & Claustre 2000; Canti 2007). Fused silica can form considerable accumulations as, for example, in the Deccan ash mounds (Allchin 1963) and burnt kraals in South Africa (Jacobson et al. 2003).
